In the criminal justice domain, she and her colleagues published a paper showing they could create a recidivism-prediction model as accurate as COMPAS that could fit into a single sentence: "If the person has more than three prior offenses, or is an 18-to-20-year-old male, or is 21-to-23 years old and has two or more priors, predict they will be rearrested; otherwise, not." Rudin looks at the simple models currently used in medicine and sees them not as superior alternatives to clinical intuition, but as models overly shaped by it, often based on arbitrary point systems made up by committees of doctors. She wants to build interpretable models directly from the data, using computational power to search the vast space of all possible simple rule lists. Finding the optimal simple rule is an "NP-hard" problem, a thicket of computational complexity. But Rudin argues that instead of using modern computing power to train massive, opaque neural networks, we should use it to exhaustively search for the best, most transparent decision rules. Her work proves that we do not have to sacrifice accuracy for interpretability; we just need better algorithms to find the simplicity that already exists in the data, offering a compelling alternative to the post-hoc explanation paradigm that dominates much of the current XAI research.

22. SLIM: Supersparse Linear Integer Models in Medical Diagnostics

Rudin's commitment to optimal simplicity was put to the test in collaboration with Berk Ustun and Massachusetts General Hospital, aiming to develop a scoring system for sleep apnea that could be seamlessly integrated into clinical workflows without the need for electronic health record integrations or complex software. The constraint was severe: the model had to be so simple that it could be used by physicians on paper notepads, requiring very few explicit features and integer coefficients as small as possible. They developed a model called SLIM (Supersparse Linear Integer Model) to find provably optimal ways to make decisions under these constraints, utilizing advanced mixed-integer programming techniques to navigate the vast space of possible models. The results were groundbreaking. First, the model revealed a counterintuitive medical insight: patient symptoms (like snoring or gasping) were significantly less useful for predicting sleep apnea than their medical histories (like past heart attacks or hypertension). Adding symptoms to the history-based model didn't improve accuracy. Second, the SLIM model matched the accuracy of state-of-the-art, complex black-box classification models, but with the benefit of full transparency. Physicians could make predictions using simple yes/no answers to a small number of clinical queries. Rudin's hypothesis—that many datasets permit predictive models that are surprisingly small, highly accurate, and highly interpretable—was proven true. By prioritizing the end-user and the necessity of transparent decision-making, SLIM demonstrated that the medical field does not need to rely on artisanal, hand-crafted scoring systems, nor does it need to surrender to the opacity of deep learning, providing a blueprint for the development of trustworthy AI in high-stakes clinical environments.

23. Saliency Maps and the Visual Inspection of Neural Networks

While Rudin and others champion the use of inherently interpretable models, there are domains where complexity is simply unavoidable, particularly in computer vision and natural language processing, where models must process raw, high-dimensional sensory data. Models that process raw sensory data—like images, audio, or text—must deal with millions of raw pixels or words, precluding the use of simple rule lists based on human abstractions. In these cases, we must rely on large, multimillion-parameter "black box" neural networks. To make these models transparent, researchers have developed techniques to "pop the hood" and visualize what the network is doing, bridging the gap between raw mathematical operations and human-understandable concepts. One of the most prominent techniques is "saliency mapping." The idea is rooted in human biology: humans have distinctly visible sclera (the whites of our eyes), which exposes where we are directing our attention, facilitating cooperation and shared understanding. Saliency methods attempt to give machines a similar transparency. If a system is looking at an image and classifying it as a dog, a saliency map generates a "heat map" highlighting the specific pixels that were most influential in making that determination, typically by computing the gradient of the output class score with respect to the input image pixels. By seeing where the model is looking, we can perform a crucial sanity check. Is it looking at the dog's snout, or is it looking at the grass in the background? Saliency methods have revealed that machine-learning systems often latch onto aspects of the training data that humans did not think were relevant, ignoring what we imagine is the critical information, and highlighting the necessity of visual debugging in the development of robust computer vision systems.

24. The Bokeh Effect and the Danger of Spurious Correlations

The practice of saliency methods has been full of surprises that highlight the unintuitive and often alien nature of neural network representations, exposing the profound danger of deploying models without rigorous visual inspection. In 2013, PhD student Will Landecker was working on a neural network trained to distinguish images with animals from those without. Using saliency techniques to see what the network was focusing on, he noticed something bizarre: the network was paying more attention to the background of the picture than the foreground. A closer look revealed that blurry backgrounds—known in photography as "bokeh"—were commonly present in images of animals, where the subject is in sharp focus and the background is artfully out of focus. Empty landscapes, by contrast, tended to be uniformly in focus. Landecker realized he hadn't trained an animal detector at all; he had trained a bokeh detector. This danger of spurious correlations was echoed in a collaboration between Stanford dermatologists Justin Ko and Roberto Novoa. They trained a state-of-the-art vision system to differentiate between benign and malignant skin patterns, achieving "dermatologist-level" accuracy. However, when they analyzed the model, they discovered it was much more likely to classify any image with a ruler in it as cancerous. Why? Medical images of malignancies are much more likely to contain a ruler for scale than images of healthy skin. The algorithm had inadvertently "learned" that rulers are malignant. These examples underscore the peril of deploying black box models without rigorous visual inspection; the model will always find the easiest shortcut to minimize the loss function, even if that shortcut is a dangerous artifact of the dataset, leading to catastrophic failures when the model is deployed in the wild where the spurious correlations do not hold.

25. Multitask Learning: Extracting More Signal from Medical Data

To combat the danger of models learning spurious shortcuts and to force neural networks to learn more robust, generalizable representations of the underlying data manifold, researchers have developed techniques like "multitask learning," which leverages the inductive bias that related tasks share underlying concepts. Rich Caruana realized that when training a neural network to predict a single outcome, like patient mortality, the model might learn to cheat by latching onto a narrow, dangerous correlation, like the asthma anomaly. However, if the network is forced to predict multiple related outcomes simultaneously—such as length of hospital stay, total bill, and need for breathing assistance—it is much harder for the model to cheat. Caruana described this as providing the model with "more signal." If a patient survives but has a half-million-dollar hospital bill and stays for a month, the model learns that this was a "high-risk zero," a patient who was incredibly sick but got lucky. By forcing the network to understand the broader context of the patient's condition across multiple dimensions, multitask learning makes the model more robust and, crucially, more transparent. If the model is only predicting mortality, an anomaly might look like a statistical blip. But if the model is simultaneously predicting mortality, cost, and morbidity, the anomalies become glaringly obvious. The asthmatics might have low mortality but astronomical bills, making it clear that they are not "low-risk" patients who should be sent home. Multitask learning forces the black box to learn a richer, more accurate representation of reality, acting as a powerful regularizer that prevents the model from overfitting to dataset artifacts and improving its generalization to unseen data.

26. Retinal Scans and the Discovery of Hidden Biological Markers

The power of multitask learning and saliency methods was spectacularly demonstrated by a team spanning Google, Verily, and the Stanford School of Medicine, showcasing the potential of AI to act not just as a predictive tool, but as a scientific instrument capable of discovering novel biological phenomena. They adapted Google’s Inception v3 network to classify images of the retina to detect diseases like diabetic retinopathy. Because the dataset contained ancillary patient data—age, sex, blood pressure, smoking status—the team decided to treat this data not as inputs, but as additional outputs for the network to predict, applying the multitask learning philosophy to force the network to learn a comprehensive representation of the patient's overall health. The results shocked the medical community: the network could almost perfectly tell a patient’s age and sex from nothing but an image of their retina. The doctors initially dismissed it as a bug, but saliency maps proved the network was genuinely looking at biological features: age was determined by looking at the blood vessels, while sex was determined by looking at the macula and the optic disc. This was not just a parlor trick; it suggested that there were sex differences in the retina that human medicine had entirely overlooked. The combination of multitask learning and saliency techniques showed that AI could not only make better predictions but could actually act as a scientific instrument, discovering new biological phenomena and pointing human doctors toward areas of the body they had never thought to examine. The black box, when properly interrogated, can reveal the hidden structure of the natural world, demonstrating that interpretability techniques can drive fundamental scientific discovery.

27. Deconvolution and the Visual Reverse-Engineering of AlexNet

While saliency maps show where a model is looking on the input image, they do not explain what the model is seeing in its internal, hidden layers, leaving the intermediate representations of deep networks largely shrouded in mystery. To understand the internal representations of a deep neural network, researchers like Matthew Zeiler and Rob Fergus developed a technique called "deconvolution," a method that allows researchers to map the intermediate-level activations of the network back into pixel space, effectively visualizing what the network is "thinking" at each layer of its hierarchy. When Zeiler applied this to AlexNet, the model that had revolutionized computer vision in 2012, he was able to see the hierarchy of features the network had learned. The first layer detected basic edges and gradients. The second layer detected complex shapes like circles and t-junctions. The third layer detected portions of objects, like eyeballs and textures. By the fifth layer, the network was responding to entire objects like starfish or typewriters. More importantly, deconvolution allowed Zeiler to diagnose flaws in the network. He noticed that some low-level parts of the network had normalized incorrectly, looking like overexposed photographs, and that other filters had gone "dead" and weren't detecting anything. By visually reverse-engineering the black box, Zeiler was able to identify exactly where the architecture was failing, leading him to found the company Clarifai and win the 2013 ImageNet competition. Deconvolution proved that the internal workings of a neural network are not an impenetrable void; they are a structured, visualizable landscape of learned concepts, providing a powerful tool for model debugging and architectural refinement.

28. DeepDream, Feature Visualization, and the Aesthetics of AI

The visualization of neural networks took a surreal, psychedelic turn with the invention of DeepDream by Google engineers Alexander Mordvintsev, Christopher Olah, and Mike Tyka, pushing the boundaries of feature visualization and revealing the latent space of deep networks in a visually striking manner. Instead of starting from an image and asking what the network sees, they started from an image (or random static) and tweaked the pixels to maximize the probability that the network assigns it a particular label, say, "dog." This created a feedback loop: if a cloud looked a little bit like a bird, the network would amplify those pixels to make it look more like a bird, which in turn made the network recognize the bird even more strongly. The resulting images were hallucinatory, fractal amalgamations of eyes, ears, and feathers. While DeepDream became famous for its artistic, grotesque aesthetic, it had profound diagnostic utility. By starting from random static and fine-tuning images to maximize a category, researchers could see exactly what the network's prototype of that category looked like. When they did this for "dumbbells," the network generated surreal, flesh-colored, disembodied arms holding weights. This revealed a critical training mishap: the network had never been shown a dumbbell without a human holding it, so it failed to distill the essence of the object itself. Feature visualization allows researchers to expose dataset biases and conceptual misunderstandings, turning the black box into a mirror that reflects the flaws and assumptions of the data it was fed, and highlighting the profound importance of dataset curation in the development of robust AI systems.

29. TCAV: Testing with Concept Activation Vectors for Human-Centric AI

While visualizations like DeepDream and saliency maps are powerful, they often speak the language of pixels and gradients, not the language of humans, creating a barrier to entry for domain experts who lack a deep understanding of machine learning internals. Been Kim, a researcher at Google Brain, recognized that interpretability is inherently a human-computer interaction problem, and that true interpretability must be defined in terms of the user's needs and conceptual understanding. "Humans think and communicate using concepts," she noted. We don't talk about pixel gradients; we talk about "stripes," "gender," or "privacy." To bridge this gap, Kim and her collaborators developed TCAV (Testing with Concept Activation Vectors). TCAV allows users to test if a neural network is using high-level, human-defined concepts to make its predictions. For example, a user can provide a few images of "stripes" and a few random images. TCAV then calculates a vector representing the concept of "stripes" in the network's internal activation space and measures how much that concept influenced the network's prediction of a "zebra." If the network is using the concept of "male" to predict "doctor," TCAV will expose this bias using the user's own terminology. Kim's work democratizes interpretability, allowing domain experts—like doctors or lawyers—to test hypotheses about the model's behavior using the concepts they understand, without needing a PhD in machine learning. TCAV shifts the paradigm from explaining the model in mathematical terms to explaining it in human terms, ensuring that the AI is aligned with human values and reasoning, and paving the way for a new era of human-centric AI development where the end-user is actively involved in the validation process.

30. The Future of Interpretable Machine Learning and Societal Alignment

The journey from Cynthia Dwork's differential privacy to Been Kim's TCAV represents a profound evolution in the field of computer science, marking a transition from the naive belief in algorithmic objectivity to a mature understanding of the complex interplay between mathematics, society, and ethics. We have moved from the belief that algorithms could be neutral, blind arbiters of truth, to the mathematical proof that they inevitably encode and amplify societal biases. We have confronted the impossibility theorem, realizing that fairness is not a mathematical puzzle to be solved, but a moral tradeoff to be negotiated, requiring explicit value judgments that cannot be outsourced to code. We have peered into the black box, using saliency, multitask learning, and deconvolution to expose the spurious correlations and hidden dangers lurking within the most powerful neural networks. And we have championed the cause of optimal simplicity, proving that we do not always need impenetrable deep learning models to achieve state-of-the-art accuracy. The future of interpretable machine learning lies in the synthesis of these insights. It requires a commitment to building models that are not just accurate, but trustworthy; not just powerful, but understandable. As AI systems become increasingly integrated into the fabric of society—dictating who gets a loan, who is paroled, and who receives medical care—the demand for transparency will only grow. The ultimate goal is not to create machines that mimic human intuition, but to create tools that augment human judgment, providing clear, explainable, and fair decisions that we can confidently stand behind. The math is the math, as Dwork says, and we are much better off knowing what the limits are than not. By embracing interpretability, we ensure that the algorithms of the future serve humanity, rather than obscuring it, forging a path toward a more equitable and transparent technological landscape.
III. The Foundations of Agency: A Comprehensive History of Reinforcement Learning, Dopamine, and Reward Shaping

References:

▪ Sutton & Barto: Reinforcement Learning: An Introduction (2nd ed.)
▪ Ng, Harada, and Russell: Policy Invariance Under Reward Transformations
▪ Wolfram Schultz: Predictive Reward Signal of Dopaminergic Neurons

1. The Evolution of Interpretability and AI Ethics

In the early years of the twenty-first century, the field of artificial intelligence was largely consumed by the pursuit of raw performance, accuracy, and scale. During this era, concerns about how these complex systems actually arrived at their conclusions were often dismissed as secondary or even irrelevant. For researchers like Kim, who found herself navigating the academic landscape in 2012, it felt as though there was hardly an audience for these profound concerns about interpretability. She observed that only a tiny handful of visionaries, including her mentors Cynthia Rudin and Julie Shah at MIT, were even vaguely interested in exploring the inner workings of machine learning models. The prevailing wisdom suggested that as long as the algorithm produced the correct output, the opaque, labyrinthine process that generated that output was of little consequence to the broader scientific community.

However, the rapid, almost exponential advancement of deep learning soon forced a dramatic paradigm shift. By 2017, the sheer scale and societal impact of AI systems had made their opacity a critical liability. Entire symposia at the field’s largest and most prestigious conferences were suddenly devoted exclusively to interpretability and explanation. Researchers who had once ignored the black-box nature of neural networks were now frantically trying to reverse-engineer them. By 2019, the CEO of Google was proudly describing the company’s work on interpretability on the biggest stage, signaling that the issue had transitioned from a niche academic curiosity to a central pillar of corporate and global AI strategy. This meteoric rise in attention was not merely a changing fashion in learning theory; it was the direct result of discovering that the power to predict and control behavior using AI carried immense, often hidden, risks.

When asked if this dramatic shift felt like vindication, Kim’s response was characteristically measured. Seemingly unwilling to allow herself a moment’s satisfaction or a feeling of vindication, she emphasized that there was still such a long way to go. Instead of basking in the glory of being proven right, she felt mostly a profound sense of responsibility. Her primary concern was to ensure that these critical issues of transparency and ethics were addressed faster than the already rapid progress and deployment of the technology itself. She recognized the urgent need to somehow get out ahead of that relentless wave of innovation, in order to make sure there weren’t any catastrophic, high-stakes problems that could arise from deploying uninterpretable systems in critical real-world scenarios.

The anecdote concludes with a poignant reflection on the nature of academic progress and human humility. As Kim and the interviewer wrapped up their conversation, she recalled a specific faculty member who, back in 2012, had scoffed at her when she mentioned her PhD work on interpretability. This senior academic had tried to steer her away from what he perceived as a dead end, a futile pursuit that would not yield significant scientific results. Yet, Kim bears no grudge, recognizing genuinely that he was giving her advice based on the prevailing norms of the time. It was a reasonable, sweet thing to do from his perspective. The ultimate irony, and a testament to the rapidly evolving nature of the field, is revealed with a grin: that very same person is now working extensively on interpretability. This story perfectly encapsulates the dynamic, humbling, and often unpredictable trajectory of scientific discovery in the age of artificial intelligence.

2. Edward Thorndike and the Law of Effect

To understand the deep roots of reinforcement learning, we must travel back to the spring of 1896, when the pioneering American author Gertrude Stein enrolled in a psychology seminar at Harvard University under the tutelage of the legendary William James. There, she would study the fascinating phenomenon of "motor automatism," which is the ability to write words on paper without thinking deliberately about them. The peer-reviewed article that resulted from this seminar would be her very first appearance in print. More significantly, what began as undergraduate psychology research would lead her directly to the trademark modernist "stream of consciousness" prose style for which she would eventually become world-famous. Stein described her classmates in James’s seminar as a "funny bunch," and one in particular was a bit of a character: he was preoccupied with incubating chickens in his apartment.

The student was Edward Thorndike, and, to be fair, turning his apartment into a makeshift chicken coop wasn’t his first idea. He originally wanted to study the mechanisms of learning in human children—and to debunk the idea of extrasensory perception while he was at it. Harvard didn’t approve the project, citing ethical or logistical concerns. So, the incubator full of chirping chicks just sort of happened as a fallback plan. For an aspiring psychologist to be tending to a flock of chicks was indeed strange at the time, as animal research had yet to come into its twentieth-century vogue. Thorndike’s own classmates thought he was a bit odd, and it may have raised the eyebrows of his peers and the hackles of his landlady. However, tending his flock was, despite being a logistical nightmare, not without its perks. When traveling in Massachusetts, Thorndike was often forced to stop at the house of some family friends, the Moultons, to warm the chicks on their stove before continuing on to Cambridge. This may have been a research necessity, though it becomes clear from his letters that Thorndike was motivated in no small part by the chance to flirt with their younger daughter, Bess, who would later become his wife.

Thorndike’s landlady in Cambridge finally declared his incubator a fire hazard and gave him an ultimatum: the chicks had to go. William James attempted to secure him a laboratory space on the Harvard campus, but the university wasn’t any more enthusiastic about the idea of farm animals in academic buildings. As a last resort, James, over the protestations of his wife, let Thorndike move the chicks, incubator and all, into the basement of his own home. James’s children, at least, appear to have been delighted by the chaotic addition to their household. This bizarre intersection of domestic life and rigorous scientific inquiry set the stage for some of the most foundational discoveries in the history of psychology. Thorndike was about to unlock the fundamental mechanisms of how living organisms learn from their environment, a process that would eventually form the bedrock of artificial intelligence.

3. The Puzzle Boxes and Early Animal Learning

In 1897, Thorndike graduated from Harvard and moved to New York City for a fellowship at Columbia University, this time working with all manner of animals. The year was frequently bleak and filled with logistical chaos. Two of his cats ran away, he couldn’t get any dogs, and he noted in his correspondence that his monkey was so wild he couldn’t touch him. To make matters worse, not all of the animals in Thorndike’s urban menagerie were there for science; a number of them were simply pests. In a letter to Bess on Valentine’s Day in 1898, he described his desolate flat, noting that a mouse had just run across his foot, rats were gnawing the bureau, and the floor was covered in tobacco ends, newspapers, coal, and a chicken pen. Yet, out of that squalor would come some of the most original and consequential research of the nineteenth century.

Thorndike had built a set of contraptions he called "puzzle boxes," full of latches, levers, and buttons. He would drop an animal inside the box, place some food outside, and observe the way that the animals—chicks, cats, and dogs—managed to find their way out. What Thorndike observed while eating apples and smoking cigarettes in his messy apartment was that the animals, who had no idea how the box actually worked, initially behaved almost purely randomly. They bit things, nudged things, and scratched at the walls. Once one of these random actions got them out of the box, they very quickly learned to repeat that action, becoming more and more capable of quickly escaping that same box in the future. "The one impulse, out of many accidental ones, which leads to pleasure," he observed, "becomes strengthened and stamped in thereby."

Thorndike saw here the makings of a bigger, more general law of nature. As he put it, the results of our actions are either "satisfying" or "annoying." When the result of an action is satisfying, we tend to do it more. When, on the other hand, the outcome is annoying, we will do it less. The more clear the connection between action and outcome, the stronger the resulting change in behavior. Thorndike called this idea, perhaps the most famous and durable of his career, "the law of effect." This seemingly modest and intuitive idea would become the foundation upon which much of twentieth-century psychology was built. In 1927, Ivan Pavlov’s Conditioned Reflexes appeared in English, translated by his student Gleb von Anrep, who used the term "reinforcement" to refer to this exact effect. By 1933, Thorndike himself was using the term reinforcement, as was a young Harvard postdoc by the name of Burrhus Frederic (B. F.) Skinner. The language of reinforcement set the frame in which much of psychology would operate for decades to come, bridging the gap between animal instinct and human cognition.

4. Digital Trial and Error: Turing's Unorganized Machines

If the animal researchers following Thorndike were ultimately interested in the psychology of the human child, they were not alone; computer scientists—the very first ones—were deeply invested in the same questions. Alan Turing’s most famous paper, "Computing Machinery and Intelligence," published in 1950, explicitly framed the project of artificial intelligence in these exact behaviorist terms. "Instead of trying to produce a programme to simulate the adult mind," he wrote, "why not rather try to produce one which simulates the child’s? If this were then subjected to an appropriate course of education one would obtain the adult brain." Turing imagined these ersatz child minds as what he called "unorganized machines," beginning in a random configuration that would then be modified by the quality of the outcomes of their initially random actions. This was a profound conceptual leap, suggesting that intelligence was not something that had to be explicitly programmed, but rather something that could emerge from interaction with an environment.

A road map to artificial intelligence was already taking shape in the mid-twentieth century. The "unorganized machines" would borrow directly from what was known about the nervous system, and the "course of education" would borrow directly from what the behaviorists were discovering about how animals and children learned. Warren McCulloch and Walter Pitts had by the early 1940s shown that a large assembly of artificial "neurons," suitably wired, was capable of computing just about anything. Turing had begun to sketch out ways that such a network might be trained through trial and error. Indeed, this was precisely the process of "stamping in" that Thorndike had described fifty years before in his smoky apartment zoo. Turing’s description of how a machine might learn from pain and pleasure stimuli resembles, nearly verbatim, Thorndike’s law of effect. When a configuration is reached for which the action is undetermined, a random choice is made and applied. When a pain stimulus occurs, all tentative entries are cancelled, and when a pleasure stimulus occurs, they are all made permanent.

This elegant symmetry between biological learning and machine computation was not lost on the pioneers of the field. They recognized that the fundamental mechanism of learning—trial and error guided by feedback—was substrate-independent. It did not matter whether the "neurons" were made of biological tissue or vacuum tubes; the mathematical principles of reinforcement remained the same. Turing’s vision laid the philosophical groundwork for what would eventually become the field of reinforcement learning. He understood that to create a truly intelligent machine, one could not simply write a list of rules for every conceivable situation. Instead, one had to create a system that could learn from its own mistakes, a system that could navigate the vast, uncharted space of possible actions and discover the path to success through its own experience. This was the seed from which the towering edifice of modern machine learning would eventually grow.

5. Arthur Samuel and the Birth of Machine Learning

By the end of the 1950s, the theoretical musings of Turing and the psychological insights of Thorndike were beginning to manifest in tangible, working software. Arthur Samuel, working out of IBM’s Poughkeepsie laboratory, had built a program to play checkers that, in a crude and early way, adjusted its own parameters on the basis of won and lost games. It wasn’t long before Samuel was losing matches to his own creation. As the New Yorker reported in 1959, "Dr. Samuel has thus become probably the first scientist in history to concede defeat by an adversary of his own devising." This was a watershed moment in the history of computing. For the first time, a machine was not just executing a fixed set of instructions; it was improving its own performance through experience. Samuel published a report called "Some Studies in Machine Learning Using the Game of Checkers," embracing the term "machine learning" to describe this revolutionary methodology.

Samuel recognized the profound implications of his work. He wrote that programming computers to learn from experience should eventually eliminate the need for much of the detailed, time-consuming programming effort that was currently required to solve complex problems. In plainer English, he explained that it was one of the most satisfying things that had ever happened to him, noting that nobody else had ever got a digital computer to improve of its own accord. The kind of mental activity that computers could simulate had always been severely limited because programmers had to tell them exactly what to do and exactly how to do it. By allowing the machine to learn, Samuel had broken through that bottleneck. The machine could now discover strategies and heuristics that its human creator had never explicitly conceived, effectively surpassing the intellectual limitations of its programmer.

The unveiling of Samuel’s research became the stuff of computer science legend. Fellow AI pioneer John McCarthy recounts that when Samuel was getting ready to demonstrate his checkers program on national television, Thomas J. Watson Sr., the founder and President of IBM, remarked that the demonstration would raise the price of IBM stock 15 points. And it did. This anecdote highlights the immense commercial and cultural anticipation surrounding the development of thinking machines. IBM stock did indeed surge, reflecting the public’s fascination with the idea that computers could transcend their role as mere calculators and become genuine problem-solvers. Samuel’s work proved that the principles of reinforcement could be instantiated in silicon, setting the stage for decades of research into algorithms that could learn to play games, control robots, and eventually, navigate the complexities of the real world. The checkers program was a humble beginning, but it contained within it the DNA of every modern AI system that learns from reward and punishment.

6. Harry Klopf and the Hedonistic Neuron

In 1972, Harry Klopf, a researcher working for the United States Air Force out of Wright-Patterson Air Force Base in Dayton, Ohio, published a provocative report titled "Brain Function and Adaptive Systems: A Heterostatic Theory." Klopf argued that "the neuron is a hedonist": one that works to maximize some approximate, local notion of "pleasure" and minimize some notion of "pain." The whole complexity of human and animal behavior, Klopf believed, arises as a result of these individual cellular hedonists wired up into systems of increasing complexity. This was a radical departure from the prevailing scientific orthodoxy of the time, which viewed biological systems primarily through the lens of equilibrium and stability. Klopf’s heterostatic theory proposed that the fundamental drive of life was not to maintain a steady state, but to constantly seek out and maximize positive stimuli.

The generation before Klopf, the so-called cybernetics movement of the 1940s through the 1960s, had framed intelligent behavior specifically in terms of what they called "negative feedback." Organisms were motivated, they argued, largely by homeostasis or equilibrium. They strove to maintain a comfortable temperature, ate to quell their hunger, mated to quell their desire, and slept to quell their fatigue. Everything seemed to be about getting back to baseline. The seminal 1943 cybernetics paper "Behavior, Purpose and Teleology," which coined the term "feedback" in its now common sense, set out to distinguish purposeful from purposeless behavior. For the cyberneticists, purpose was tantamount to a goal that could be arrived at as a place of rest. For arch-cyberneticist Norbert Wiener, one of the canonical "intrinsically purposeful" machines is a thermostat: when the temperature is too low, it turns on the heat, and when the temperature gets high enough, it shuts it off. He thought also of the "governor" of an engine, which opens valves when the engine is running too fast and closes them when it’s running too slow, helping it to maintain equilibrium. Notice that the feedback tends to oppose what the system is already doing, and is thus negative. This was an essential part of any goal-oriented system in the cybernetic view.

Klopf was having none of it. For him, organisms were maximizers, not minimizers. Life was about growth, reproduction, endless and boundless and insatiable forward progress in any number of senses. For Klopf, the goal was not homeostasis at all, but the opposite. "Living adaptive systems seek, as their primary goal, a maximal condition (heterostasis), rather than... a steady-state condition (homeostasis)." More poetically, he espoused the virtues of positive, rather than negative feedback: "Both positive and negative feedback are essential to life’s processes. However, it is positive feedback that is the dominant force—it provides the ‘spark of life.’" This notion ran all the way through from single cells to organisms to societies. Klopf was not modest about what he saw as the implications of this idea. He believed it was the first theory capable of providing a single unifying framework within which the neurophysiological, psychological, and sociological properties of living adaptive systems could be understood. Neurons, nervous systems, and nations were all heterostats, constantly striving for more, never satisfied with mere equilibrium. This bold, unorthodox idea would soon attract the attention of the Air Force and lay the groundwork for a revolution in artificial intelligence.

7. Heterostasis vs. Homeostasis: The Spark of Life

The proposition that neurons were insatiable maximizers, and that this explained the behavior of nations, was an ambitious, unorthodox, and quite possibly absurd idea. Yet, the Air Force offered Klopf funding to find or assemble a lab of researchers to look into it. He found his team at the University of Massachusetts Amherst. There he hired a postdoctoral researcher named Andrew Barto to determine, as Barto puts it, "the scientific significance of this idea: was it a crazy, crackpot idea, or did it have some scientific merit?" This was the crucible in which the modern field of reinforcement learning would be forged. Barto, with his rigorous mathematical background, was the perfect person to test the boundaries of Klopf’s heterostatic theory. He approached the problem not as a philosopher, but as a scientist determined to extract the underlying computational principles of biological learning.

Klopf had, perhaps unusually, been keeping a running correspondence during this time with a bright undergraduate psychology major from Stanford. When Barto came onboard, Klopf encouraged him: "There’s this really bright kid, you should get him into this project." The bright kid was named Richard Sutton; still a teenager, he would join Barto in Amherst and become Barto’s very first graduate student. This fateful introduction brought together two brilliant minds who would spend the next half-century unraveling the mysteries of learning. I ask Barto if there was any foreshadowing of what was to come. "We had no idea," he tells me. "We had no idea." Sutton and Barto would, with Klopf’s Air Force grant, begin a forty-five-year collaboration that would essentially found a new field. The field, which would cross neuroscience, behaviorist psychology, engineering, and mathematics, was dubbed "reinforcement learning." Their names, forever linked in bibliographies of AI as "Barto and Sutton" or "Sutton and Barto," would become synonymous with the definitive textbook of the field they coauthored, and indeed, almost a shorthand for the field of reinforcement learning itself.

The transition from Klopf’s biological hypothesis to Barto and Sutton’s mathematical framework was a monumental intellectual achievement. They took the vague, poetic notion of the "hedonistic neuron" and the "spark of life" and translated it into the precise, unyielding language of algorithms and probability. They realized that if organisms were indeed maximizers, then the problem of learning could be formalized as an optimization problem. The environment provides a scalar signal—a reward—and the organism’s goal is to discover the sequence of actions that maximizes the cumulative sum of that signal over time. This simple yet profound abstraction allowed them to bypass the messy details of neurobiology and focus on the universal computational principles of adaptive behavior. In doing so, they created a framework that was not only applicable to rats and pigeons but to any system, biological or artificial, that must learn to navigate a complex and uncertain world. The heterostatic spark had been captured, and it was about to illuminate the path to artificial general intelligence.

8. The Genesis of Reinforcement Learning: Barto and Sutton

Barto and Sutton took Harry Klopf’s idea of organisms as maximizers and gave it a concrete, mathematical form. Imagine you are in an environment that contains rewards of some numerical value, which are available to you if you take certain actions and reach certain states. Your job is to get the most reward—the highest "score"—that you can. This framework is incredibly versatile. The environment could be a maze, your actions could be moving north, south, east, or west, and your reward could come from reaching the exit, perhaps minus a small time penalty. Or the environment could be a chessboard, your actions the moving of the pieces, and your reward one point for checkmating your opponent and half a point for reaching a draw. Or perhaps the environment is the stock market, your actions are buying and selling, and the reward is measured in the dollar value of your portfolio. There is virtually no limit to the complexity of these scenarios. The environment could be a national economy, the actions could be issuing words of legislation or diplomacy, and the reward could be long-term GDP growth, as long as the rewards are what is known as scalar: they are commensurate, fungible, and of a common currency.

The reinforcement-learning framework has proven to be so general, in fact, that it has led to an idea known as the "reward hypothesis": "That all of what we mean by goals and purposes can be well thought of as the maximization of the cumulative sum of a received scalar reward." This is almost a philosophical thing, as Richard Sutton notes, but you kind of have to buy into it to make progress in the field. Of course, not everyone buys into this premise so readily. In sports, in board games and video games, in finance, there may indeed be such a scalar number, a single currency for measuring all outcomes, but can that really be applied to something as complex and multiform as human, or even animal, life? We often have to make decisions whose outcomes seem like apples and oranges. Do we work late, improving our standing with our boss but testing the patience of our spouse? Do we prioritize a life of achievement, a life of adventure, a life of human connection, or a life of spiritual growth? Oxford University philosopher Ruth Chang has spent decades arguing that nothing so characterizes the human condition as the incommensurability of the various motives and goals we have. We cannot simply put major life choices on a scale and weigh out which one comes out best, or else there would be nothing to morality but pure reason, no opportunity to make meaning, to forge an identity.

Sutton himself concedes that the reward hypothesis is "probably ultimately wrong, but so simple we have to disprove it before considering anything more complicated." This pragmatic approach is the hallmark of great scientific progress. By assuming a simplified model of the world, researchers can derive powerful insights and build functional systems, even if the model is not a perfect reflection of reality. The reward hypothesis provides a common language for discussing goals, learning, and decision-making across vastly different domains. It allows a neuroscientist studying dopamine in monkeys to communicate effectively with a computer scientist training a robot to walk. Even if human motivation is ultimately more complex than a single scalar reward, the reinforcement learning framework captures the essential mechanics of how any adaptive system learns to achieve its objectives. It is a testament to the power of abstraction in science: by stripping away the messy details, we can uncover the fundamental laws that govern complex behavior.

9. The Reward Hypothesis and Scalar Maximization

Even if we accept, provisionally, the framework of reward maximization and the commensurability of scalar rewards, we find that being a heterostatic maximizer is harder than it sounds. Indeed, the problem of reinforcement learning is fraught with difficulty both philosophical and mathematical. The first challenge is that our decisions are connected. Here reinforcement learning is subtly, but importantly, different from both unsupervised learning, which builds vector word representations, and supervised learning, which is used for everything from image classification to recidivism risk assessment. In those settings, every decision stands alone. Your system is shown a picture, say, a chanterelle mushroom, and asked to categorize it. The system may get the answer right, it may get it wrong, its parameters may be fine-tuned a little bit in the event of a mistake, but in any case you simply pull another photo at random from your collection and move on. The data coming in as input are what statisticians call "i.i.d.": independent and identically distributed. There is no causal connection between what we see, what we do, and what we see next.

In reinforcement learning, whether in a maze, in a chess game, or indeed in life, we don’t have the luxury of making our decisions in a vacuum. Every decision we make sets the context in which our next decision will be made, and, in fact, it may change that context permanently. If we develop our chess pieces a certain way, we are strongly constraining the types of positions we will encounter and the types of strategies that may be useful going forward. In a spatial world, whether virtual or real, it is intrinsic to locomotion that the actions we take sculpt the future input we receive, either temporarily or forever. If we sacrifice our queen, it is unavailable to us for the rest of the game. If we jump off the roof, we may never jump again. If we are unkind to someone, we may forever alter how that person behaves toward us, and we may never know how they might have behaved had we been kinder. This interconnectedness means that the agent must not only consider the immediate consequences of its actions but also their long-term ramifications. It must learn to anticipate the future states of the world that its current actions will bring about, a cognitive feat that requires a sophisticated form of planning and foresight.

This temporal dimension of decision-making is what makes reinforcement learning both incredibly difficult and incredibly powerful. It mirrors the fundamental nature of biological life, where every action has consequences that ripple forward in time. An animal that eats all its food today may starve tomorrow; a human that spends all their money today may face ruin in the future. The ability to delay gratification, to sacrifice immediate reward for long-term gain, is a hallmark of intelligent behavior. Reinforcement learning provides the mathematical tools to model and solve these temporal credit assignment problems. It forces the agent to develop a concept of the future, to value not just the present moment but the trajectory of states that leads to ultimate success. In doing so, it captures the essence of what it means to be an agent in the world: a being that acts not just in response to immediate stimuli, but in pursuit of distant goals. The connectedness of decisions is not just a computational hurdle; it is the very fabric of purposeful behavior.

10. The Credit Assignment Problem in Complex Environments

The second challenge of reinforcement learning, relative to supervised and unsupervised learning, is that the rewards or punishments we get from the environment, owing to their very scalar quality, are terse. A language model trained to predict a missing word is told, after each guess, what the correct word was. An image classifier attempting to classify an image is immediately given the "correct" label for that image. It then updates itself explicitly in the direction of that correct answer. In contrast, a reinforcement-learning system, trying its best to maximize some quantity in some environment, eventually comes to learn what score it achieved, but it may never know, win or lose, what the "correct" or "best" actions should have been. When a rocket blows up, or a bridge collapses, or a pile of plates you’re trying to carry topples, or the ball you kick fails to go into the net, the world is crystal clear about the result. But it’s mum on exactly what you should have done differently.

As Andrew Barto puts it, reinforcement learning is less like learning with a teacher than learning with a critic. The critic may be every bit as wise, but is far less helpful. The teacher would look over your shoulder as you work, correcting your mistakes instantly, and telling you or showing you exactly what you should be doing instead. The critic waits until your labor is done, then shouts "Boo!" from the back of a dark auditorium, leaving you clueless as to what exactly they didn’t like, or what they’d have preferred. The critic may not even be withholding anything by way of insight or constructive feedback; they may not themselves be left with anything other than their degree of, in Thorndike’s terms, "satisfaction or annoyance." This lack of explicit instruction means that the agent must figure out the causal structure of the environment on its own. It must infer which of its many actions contributed to the final outcome, a process that is fraught with uncertainty and requires massive amounts of data and computation.

Third, not only is feedback terse and not especially constructive, it’s delayed. We may make an unrecoverable blunder on the fifth move of a game, for instance, in which the coup de grâce comes a hundred moves later. The idiom that comes most to mind when someone experiences a failure or defeat is "Where did I go wrong?" In reinforcement learning, this is known as the "credit-assignment problem," and it had been vexing researchers since the midcentury. MIT’s Marvin Minsky, for instance, wrote in his famous 1961 paper "Steps Toward Artificial Intelligence," "In playing a complex game such as chess or checkers, or in writing a computer program, one has a definite success criterion—the game is won or lost. But in the course of play, each ultimate success (or failure) is associated with a vast number of internal decisions. If the run is successful, how can we assign credit for the success among the multitude of decisions?" Minsky elaborated, driving home the point: "Suppose that one million decisions are involved in a complex task (such as winning a chess game). Could we assign to each decision... one-millionth of the credit for the completed task?" This is the fundamental puzzle of reinforcement learning: how to distribute the blame or praise for a distant outcome across a long sequence of preceding actions. Solving this problem is the key to unlocking the ability of machines to master complex, multi-step tasks.

11. The Riddle of Dopamine: Pleasure Centers in the Brain

If humans and other animals can be thought of as "heterostatic" reward maximizers, it stands to reason that those rewards operate by way of some mechanism in the brain. If there really is some kind of singular, scalar "reward" that humans and animals are designed to maximize, might it be as simple as a chemical or a circuit in the brain? In the 1950s, a pair of researchers at McGill University in Montreal, James Olds and Peter Milner, appeared—tantalizingly—to have found its location. Olds and Milner were experimenting with placing electrodes in various places in the brains of rats, and giving the rats an opportunity to press a lever that would deliver electrical current through those electrodes to that particular part of the brain. Some areas of the brain, they discovered, seemed to have no effect on the rats’ behavior. In other areas, the rats would appear to go out of their way to make sure the lever did not get pressed. But there were some areas, the so-called "septal area" in particular, where rats appeared to want almost nothing more than to press the lever that delivered current to that area.

The rats pressed the lever as many as 5,000 times an hour, and for as long as 24 hours without rest. "The control exercised over the animal’s behavior by means of this reward is extreme," Olds and Milner wrote, "possibly exceeding that exercised by any other reward previously used in animal experimentation." The foundation had been laid for a study, not only of human and animal behavior through the lens of reward maximization, but of the actual molecular mechanisms of reward itself. At first these areas were dubbed "reinforcing structures," but soon Olds began referring to them as "pleasure centers." Subsequent research showed that not just rats but humans, too, would go out of their way to receive electrical current in similar parts of their brain. This was a stunning discovery. It suggested that the complex, subjective experience of pleasure could be reduced to the stimulation of a specific, localized neural circuit. If you could find the "pleasure center," you could theoretically control behavior with absolute precision, bypassing all the messy psychological and environmental variables.

In time, studies began to establish that the areas of the brain in which this electrical stimulation was most compelling were those areas involving neurons that produced a neurotransmitter called 3,4-dihydroxyphenethylamine, better known by its abbreviated nickname: dopamine. These cells were rare, much less than 1% of 1% of the brain, and they clustered in very specific areas. A single one of these dopamine cells could be wired in some cases to millions of other neurons, across a wide area of the brain. In fact, they were almost uniquely broadly connected, with the most highly connected cells each having nearly fifteen feet of axonal wiring within the brain. At the same time, however, they were fairly limited in the range and complexity of their output. As NYU neuroscientist Paul Glimcher puts it, this means that "they cannot say much to the rest of the brain, but what they say must be widely heard." It sounded a lot, in other words, like a "reward scalar," in essence, like points on the brain’s scoreboard, dead simple, but widely broadcast and all-important. Dopamine also appeared to be intimately involved in drugs of addiction, including cocaine, heroin, and alcohol. Could it be that dopamine was literally the molecular currency of reward in the brain?

12. Wolfram Schultz and the Monkey Juice Experiments

Work in the late 1970s appeared to suggest as much. A study in 1978, for instance, showed that when rats were given the dopamine-blocking drug pimozide before being taught that pressing a lever would give them food pellets, they showed no more interest in pressing the lever than rats who had never learned the food connection at all. Somehow the food reward just didn’t seem to have an effect on the rats treated with pimozide. Pimozide, wrote the researchers, "appears to selectively blunt the rewarding impact of food and other hedonic stimuli." As neuroscientist Roy Wise wrote in 1982, it was as if "all of life’s pleasures—the pleasures of primary reinforcement and the pleasures of their associated stimuli—lose the ability to arouse the animal." In fact, when given drugs known to block dopamine receptors, the rats stopped delivering electrical current to their brains as well. The drug, in the words of neuroscientist George Fouriezos, took "the jolts out of the volts." It was as if everything, from food and water to sex to self-administered current, had lost its pleasurable effect. This strongly supported the hypothesis that dopamine was the essential chemical mediator of reward.

Over the 1980s, electrophysiology began advancing to the point that individual dopamine neurons could be monitored in real time. The German neurophysiologist Wolfram Schultz, at a lab in Fribourg, Switzerland, began studying the behavior of dopamine neurons in monkeys as they reached into a box that was sometimes empty and other times contained tiny pieces of fruit or baked goods. Sure enough, a burst of impulses occurred when the monkey’s hand touched a morsel of food inside the box. This seemed to confirm that scientists had, in fact, found the chemical of reward. The dopamine neurons were firing in direct response to the receipt of a primary reinforcer, exactly as the pleasure center hypothesis would predict. It was a beautiful, elegant confirmation of decades of psychological theory. The brain had a reward system, and dopamine was its currency.

But there was something strange. In cases where there was a visual or auditory cue of some kind that indicated when food had appeared in the box, the cue triggered a rush of dopamine activity. Then the monkeys would reach in and grab their food, while Schultz dutifully monitored the readings, and nothing happened. Just the calm background static of their normal baseline of activity. No spike. The dopamine neurons, he wrote, "discharged a burst of impulses in response to the cue but entirely failed to respond to the touch of food." What in the world was going on? If dopamine was the reward chemical, why did it stop firing when the actual reward was received? Schultz rifled through several hypotheses. Maybe the monkeys were simply satiated, and didn’t really want any more food. He tried starving them. It didn’t work. They ate the food ravenously. But there was no dopamine spike. The data simply did not fit the simple "dopamine equals reward" model. The riddle of dopamine was just beginning, and its solution would require a radical rethinking of how the brain processes value and expectation.

13. Temporal Difference Learning: Guessing from Guesses

Over that same period in the 1980s while Schultz was beginning his experiments on the dopamine system, on the other side of the Atlantic, Barto and Sutton were starting to make mathematical progress on the reinforcement-learning problem. The first big step was to decompose the problem. Learning how to take actions within an environment to maximize reward, they realized, involved two related but potentially independent subproblems: action and estimation. As you began to understand and ultimately master a domain, you learned two important things: how to take the right action in a given situation, and how to estimate the likely future rewards that a state of affairs might hold. A chess player looking at the board has two distinct hunches. She has a certain instinct for which moves suggest themselves to be played next, and she has another instinct for which player in this position is likely to win. These two dimensions of the reinforcement-learning problem have come to be known by the technical terms of the policy (what to do when) and the value function (what rewards or punishments to expect).

Either of these alone was, at least in theory, enough to solve the problem. If a chess player always knew the right move to play, then it didn’t matter if they were lousy at predicting who would win. The reverse, however, was also true: if a chess player always knew exactly who stood better in a given position, then it didn’t matter if they weren’t sure what to do. Given enough time, they could simply weigh the consequences of each move, and choose the one that led to the most promising future. Policy-based approaches led to a system with highly trained "muscle memory." The right behavior just flowed effortlessly. Value-based approaches, by contrast, led to a system with a highly trained "spider-sense." It could tell right away if a situation was threatening or promising. In practice, however, policy-based approaches and value-based approaches went hand in hand. Barto and Sutton began to elaborate an idea known as the "actor-critic" architecture, where the "actor" half of the system would learn to take good actions, and the "critic" half would learn to predict future rewards.

Going back all the way to his undergraduate thesis in 1978, Sutton had been preoccupied with creating a "Unified Theory of Expectation." This fascination with how organisms form and refine their expectations followed him to Amherst in his work with Klopf and Barto. As Sutton reasoned, developing good expectations meant reconciling your moment-to-moment expectations with the ultimate verdict that came from reality. But if you actually had to wait until the end of a game to learn from it, then the credit-assignment problem would indeed be virtually impossible. The breakthrough that Sutton made in thinking about predictions is this: as you move forward toward an uncertain future, you maintain a kind of "running expectation" of how promising things seem. These guesses fluctuate over time, and in general they get more accurate the closer you are to whatever it is you’re trying to predict. This means that, in general, as our expectation fluctuates, we get differences between our successive expectations, each of which is a learning opportunity; Sutton called these temporal differences, or TD errors. Any time our expectation changes can be treated as an error in our previous estimate, and, hence, an opportunity to learn: not from the ultimate truth, which has yet to arrive, but from the new estimate, made by our very slightly older and wiser self. As Sutton puts it: "We are learning a guess from a guess."

14. TD-Gammon and the Triumph of Self-Taught Machines

By the end of the 1980s, this idea, which Sutton called "the method of temporal differences," or "TD learning," for short, had sharpened into an algorithm, which he called "TD(lambda)," for precisely tuning predictions in the light of subsequent predictions. Inspired by Sutton and Barto’s ideas, Cambridge PhD student Chris Watkins had devised a TD algorithm called "Q-learning," which would turn these predictions into actions. Promisingly, he showed that Q-learning would always "converge," namely, as long as the system had the opportunity to try every action, from every state, as many times as necessary, it would always, eventually develop the perfect value function: the perfect set of expectations for that environment. It was a significant theoretical milestone for the field. The theory looked good, but TD learning had yet to be truly tested in practice. That first real kicking of the tires would happen at IBM Research in New York.

A young researcher named Gerald Tesauro, initially interested in computational models of classical conditioning, had been working, over the late 1980s and early 1990s, on using neural networks to play backgammon. The early results were promising. Then, in 1992, he plugged TD learning into his model, and it took off like a rocket. It was learning guesses from guesses, steadily coming to learn what an advantageous position looked like. "This is apparently the first application of this algorithm to a complex non-trivial task," Tesauro wrote. "It is found that, with zero knowledge built in, the network is able to learn from scratch to play the entire game at a fairly strong intermediate level of performance, which is clearly better than conventional commercial programs, and which in fact surpasses comparable networks trained on a massive human expert dataset. This indicates that TD learning may work better in practice than one would expect based on current theory." By 1994, his program, dubbed "TD-Gammon," had reached a truly staggering level of performance: the latest version of TD-Gammon was extremely close to the world’s best human players.

The field had long known that it was possible for a programmer to write a system that played better than him- or herself; Arthur Samuel had discovered as much with checkers in the 1950s. But this was something new: a program as good as any human player. And it was entirely "self-taught": initialized from a random state, and tuned over countless games against itself. It was an enormous validation for TD learning. And it would offer a direct blueprint for success that would be used, with surprisingly few changes, by twenty-first-century game-mastering software like AlphaZero. Sutton and others in the burgeoning field of reinforcement learning had taken nature’s hints. But now it was their turn to offer something back. In one of the most stunning moments in the field’s history, the mathematical framework that had grown initially out of psychology and neuroscience was about to reenter those fields in a big way. The abstract mathematics of temporal difference had conquered the backgammon board, proving that machines could achieve superhuman performance through pure, unadulterated self-play and reinforcement.

15. The Neural Substrate of Prediction and Reward

In the early 1990s, as Tesauro was fine-tuning his backgammon system, a young cognitive scientist named Peter Dayan, who had spent fertile time in Barto’s lab and had worked with Watkins on his convergence proof, found himself moving from one side of the field, and the world, to the other. From studying the mathematics of temporal difference at the University of Edinburgh, he landed with a group of neuroscientists at the Salk Institute in San Diego. Dayan and a fellow postdoc named Read Montague, working with Salk Institute neuroscientist Terry Sejnowski, had a hunch that not only did the framework of reinforcement learning explain how real human and animal brains might operate, but that it might literally be what brains were doing. "We went after the role of a set of systems in your brain that report on value and reinforcement," says Montague. "And we, at the time, just sort of thought, ‘These things should be implementing a certain kind of algorithm.’" Montague thought that these sorts of learning mechanisms might be one of the bedrock properties of almost every animal that learns. Dayan was coming from his work on temporal-difference learning, which seemed like it might be a candidate for one of these universal algorithms. It worked on the whiteboard, it worked in machines, perhaps it worked in brains, too.

The two of them began speculating about how a neurological system for learning from temporal differences might work. "Obviously we knew there must be a link to neuroscience," Dayan explains. "And then," he says, "we came across these data from Wolfram Schultz." Suddenly Dayan hops up and begins scrawling dopamine-response diagrams on the whiteboard excitedly. He points to the flat, background static, ho-hum response when the monkey gets the juice reward it fully expects after seeing the cue light. "So this part of the signal is very transparent. And that’s what you see in the Rescorla-Wagner rule; that’s the beautiful aspect of what comes from older psychology." Then he points to the initial dopamine spike caused by the cue light itself. "But it’s this part of the signal which is confusing," he says. "Because that is not expected from the psychological perspective. So at various times you can see Wolfram sort of struggling with what that means."

When Dayan and Montague looked at the data from Schultz, the data that Schultz, along with the entire neurophysiology community, found so inscrutable, they knew exactly what it meant. It was a temporal difference. It was a sudden fluctuation in the monkey’s expectation, in its value function, its prediction of how good a state of affairs it was in. A sudden spike above the brain’s dopamine background chatter meant that suddenly the world seemed more promising than it had a moment ago. A sudden hush, on the other hand, meant that suddenly things seemed less promising than imagined. The normal background static meant that things, however good or bad they were, were as good or bad as expected. A spike in the dopamine system was not reward as such, but it was related to reward; it wasn’t uncertainty, or surprise, or attention per se, but it was intimately, and for the first time legibly, related to all of them. It was a fluctuation in the monkey’s expectation, indicating that an earlier prediction had been in error; it was its brain learning a guess from a guess. The algorithm that worked so well on paper, and so well in silicon, had just been found in the brain. Temporal-difference learning didn’t just resemble the function of dopamine. It was the function of dopamine.

16. Bridging Neuroscience and Artificial Intelligence

Schultz, Dayan, and Montague published an explosive paper together in Science in 1997, announcing their discovery to the world. They had found, as they put it, "A Neural Substrate of Prediction and Reward." The effect, across neuroscience and computer science alike, was huge. An idea developed in a pure machine-learning context, inspired by models of classical and operant conditioning from psychology, had suddenly come full circle. It wasn’t just a model of how artificial intelligence might be structured. It appeared to offer a description of one of the universal principles for intelligence, period. "It’s thought that eyes evolved anywhere from sort of forty to fifty separate times... Biology has discovered eyes over and over again, all kinds of different eyes," Montague explains. "I think it’s the same thing in the learning domain. Learning algorithms are so profoundly important to understand how to take experience now, reorganize your internal structure, and produce actions in the future, that... you should expect that these algorithms have been stumbled into by biology in many different contexts. And so you see reinforcement learning systems, reward systems, in bees, and sea slugs, and birds learning songs, and human beings, and rodents, etc."

Dayan sees it the same way. "It’s no surprise that we come endowed with these sorts of things," he says. "The idea that you would see it quite so transparently in the activity of these dopamine neurons... That was quite a revelation." Visiting Wolfram Schultz’s lab in Cambridge, at age 75, he is still as animated and energized by his work as ever. I ask him if this felt like a revelation at the time. Surprisingly, he says no: the revelation, in his view, is that the humble TD-learning model, which described the activity in the brains of his monkeys as they fumbled for food, is, twenty years later, enabling the current contemporary breakthroughs of AI. That we’ve been able to take these universal ideas from nature and implement them synthetically is what blows his mind. "The revelation came," he says, "when I realized that the TD model goes into what we now see, the Go programming, and this artificial intelligence, machine-learning thing. That was a revelation, where I said, My God, what have I done? You know, understanding my data came from the Rescorla-Wagner model, from a prediction error, but the consequence, like, Jesus Christ! I mean, we knew Tesauro was programming backgammon with a TD model. Okay, I’m not a backgammon player, but I’m a Go player. Not a very good one, but... I said, dammit, if they can program Go, which has been a problem before, it’s a really good model. And I saw that before they programmed Go."

The effect on neuroscience has been transformative. As Princeton’s Yael Niv puts it, "The potential advantages of understanding learning and action selection at the level of dopamine-dependent function of the basal ganglia can not be exaggerated: dopamine is implicated in a huge variety of disorders ranging from Parkinson’s disease, through schizophrenia, major depression, attentional deficit hyperactive disorder etc., and ending in decision-making aberrations such as substance abuse and addiction." She recounts her first time attending the annual Society for Neuroscience meeting. "I remember looking up reinforcement learning the first time I went, which was probably 2003, 2004. There were maybe five posters in the whole meeting... Now," she says, "every poster session has a whole subsection, a whole row, about this. It’s really come a long way in ten, fifteen years." She adds, "There were lots of studies kind of testing predictions of the theory in the brain and seeing, ‘It seems like, gosh, these neurons read the textbook. They read the Sutton and Barto textbook. They know exactly what they need to do.’" The bridge between the silicon of the computer and the carbon of the brain had been built, and it was paved with the mathematics of temporal difference.

17. Happiness, Error, and the Hedonic Treadmill

If the TD-learning model has revealed the function of dopamine in the brain, not as the brain’s currency of reward, not its expectation of future rewards, but its error in its expectation of future rewards, then it leaves some questions open. In particular, it leaves open the connection to the subjective experience of pleasure and happiness. If elevated levels of dopamine signal something to the effect of "things are going to be better than I thought they were going to be," then that feeling is, itself, pleasurable. And you can see how humans and animals alike would go out of their way to get that feeling, including by way of direct chemical and electrical stimulation of dopamine neurons. You can also begin to see how artificially elevating dopamine levels produces an inevitable crash. Thinking that things will be better than you thought they would be only works for so long. Eventually you realize they’re not better than you thought they would be, and the dopamine chatter hushes. It’s possible, in effect, for dopamine to write checks that the environment can’t cash. Those checks eventually, inevitably bounce. Then your value function must, inevitably, come back down to earth.

This, of course, is the classic experience of dopamine-related drugs, cocaine being a prototypical example. The drug works in large part by inhibiting the brain’s reuptake of dopamine, leading to a temporary "flood" of it. The TD story suggests that the brain interprets this as a pervasive sense that things are going to be great, but the dopamine is writing checks that the environmental rewards can’t cash. Eventually the predicted greatness doesn’t come, and the equal and opposite negative prediction error is sure to follow. "It seemed like everything was going to be so great..." We can chemically fool our brain’s prediction mechanism, but not forever. As the writer David Lenson puts it, "Cocaine promises the greatest pleasure ever known in just a minute more... But that future never comes. There is a physical pleasure to the drug, to be sure, but it is incidental, trivial, compared to what is always just about to happen." Understanding cocaine as a drug of dopamine, and dopamine as a chemical of temporal difference, makes the story clear. By artificially dumping the brain’s supply, one experiences not the bliss that things are great but the giddy euphoria that things are surprisingly promising. If that promise isn’t kept, the temporal-difference error swings the other way, and our dopamine system goes silent. It was our high expectations that were in error. We were duped.

The connection between dopamine and the subjective experiences of happiness and pleasure is still being worked out. University College London’s Robb Rutledge has developed a mathematical model of happiness that explicitly involves dopamine. Rutledge, working with Peter Dayan and a group of collaborators at UCL, devised an experiment in which people would be making various bets and accumulating a sum of money while periodically being asked, "How happy are you right now?" They modeled the task using the mathematical tools of reinforcement learning, in order to tease apart how much money the subject had made so far, how much in total they anticipated making, and whether they had recently been pleasantly or unpleasantly surprised. Rutledge’s findings were illuminating. The first was that happiness was fleeting. However happy it made you to receive a win in a particular bet, five bets later, 92% of that impact had disappeared. Happiness did seem at least partly determined by how much subjects expected to make, but what seemed most crucial, however, was the violation of those expectations. As Rutledge writes, "Momentary happiness is a state that reflects not how well things are going but instead whether things are going better than expected." This sounds exactly like a temporal-difference error. Zooming out, we begin to get a neuroscientific and computational account of the well-known phenomena of what’s called the "hedonic treadmill." Namely, people have a stubborn and persistent return to their emotional baseline, regardless of changes in their long-term quality of life. If happiness comes not from things having gone well, but from things going better than expected, then as long as our expectations keep tuning themselves to reality, a long-term state of being pleasantly surprised should be simply unsustainable.

18. B.F. Skinner and the Art of Shaping Behavior

The links between dopamine, TD errors, and happiness have caused some researchers to begin pondering whether there are ethical implications for the subjective happiness of reinforcement-learning agents. Brian Tomasik of the Foundational Research Institute has pondered at some length the question of whether reinforcement-learning programs have moral standing. His answer is a tentative and limited yes: to the degree that they are built on similar principles as animal and human brains, there may well be some nonzero ethical consideration that they are owed. Subsequent work by others has gone so far as to explicitly define the "happiness" of a reinforcement-learning agent by its TD errors. By this logic, they note, "agents that know the world perfectly have zero expected happiness." Indeed, if dopaminergic "happiness" comes in large part from being pleasantly surprised, from having opportunities to better learn what to expect, then complete mastery of any domain seems necessarily correlated with boredom. This suggests an evolutionary and computational story about the hedonic treadmill. If our subjective happiness is intimately tied to being pleasantly surprised and it’s the nature of intelligence to work tirelessly to anticipate and mitigate surprise, then we can see how happiness of this kind becomes fleeting. We can also see the evolutionary advantage. An infant may be delighted at the mere ability to wave their arm on command. For an adult, this ability no longer carries the thrill it did in infancy. Though we may bemoan the restlessness this engenders in adults, the insatiability, this is all part of the curriculum of life. If basic motor skills were enough to thrill us indefinitely, we’d never make it to adulthood at all.

As Andrew Barto puts it, this fleetingness was something Klopf anticipated back in the early 1970s. "His point was a [homeostatic] stabilization mechanism, it tries to reduce the discrepancy to zero, and when it’s zero, it’s happy, it stops. The kind of system he wanted was never happy.
