So it’s this incessant exploration." The connection between reinforcement learning, dopamine, happiness, and exploration, as well as addiction, is something we’ll return to in later chapters. Reinforcement learning, rooted very consciously in the animal-learning studies of the early twentieth century, flourished in the abstract, mathematical world of machine learning in the 1970s and 1980s, only to triumphantly burst back into animal behavior literature with a nearly perfect model that has become the accepted story for the role of dopamine in the brain. This model, in turn, has given us deeper insight into human motivation and human happiness. Meanwhile, neuroscientific evidence, as recently as 2018, is beginning to suggest that the crazy hypothesis of Harry Klopf, that neurons are "hedonists," motivated by their own individual Thorndikean laws of effect, may not be so far off after all. "I think that neuroscience is getting close to something that is very similar to what Klopf was proposing," says Barto, with some pride on behalf of his late former mentor.

Reinforcement learning also offers us a powerful, and perhaps even universal, definition of what intelligence is. If intelligence is, as computer scientist John McCarthy famously said, "the computational part of the ability to achieve goals in the world," then reinforcement learning offers a strikingly general toolbox for doing so. Indeed it is likely that its core principles were stumbled into by evolution time and again, and it is likely that they will form the bedrock of whatever artificial intelligences the twenty-first century has in store. In some ways, though, a deeper understanding of the ability of animals and machines to achieve goals in the world has kicked the more profound philosophical can down the road. This theory, pointedly, does not tell us what we value, or what we ought to value. Dopamine, in this way, is every bit as mysterious now as when its role was lesser known. If it represents a scalar prediction error, it hides a vast realm of complexity in how that prediction is "measured." Dopamine is mum on this point. Meanwhile, we take up another question. Reinforcement learning in its classical form takes for granted the structure of the rewards in the world and asks the question of how to arrive at the behavior, the "policy," that maximally reaps them. But in many ways this obscures the more interesting, and more dire, matter that faces us at the brink of AI. We find ourselves rather more interested in the exact opposite of this question: Given the behavior we want from our machines, how do we structure the environment’s rewards to bring that behavior about? This is the alignment problem, in the context of a reinforcement learner.

19. Project Pigeon and the Mechanics of Successive Approximations

In 1943, B. F. Skinner was working on a secret wartime project, initially sponsored by the consumer food company General Mills. General Mills had given Skinner the top floor of its Gold Medal Flour mill in Minneapolis to build a laboratory. The project was one of the more audacious ones entertained during that time: Skinner and his lab were going to teach pigeons how to peck at images of bomb targets, then put the birds, in groups of three, inside of actual bombs to steer them as they dropped. "My colleagues and I knew," said Skinner, "that, in the eyes of the world, we were crazy." Skinner was aware that many would see the project as not only insane but cruel. To the first point, he noted that humans have a long and storied history of putting animals’ senses to human purposes. To the second point, he argued, "The ethical question of our right to convert a lower creature into an unwitting hero is a peacetime luxury." Skinner had long been working on the study of reinforcement, and his famous "Skinner boxes" functioned like upgraded, mid-twentieth-century versions of Thorndike’s puzzle boxes. Their lights and levers and mechanical food dispensers allowed for a precise and quantitative study of reinforcement.

With such tools at his disposal in the 1950s, Skinner began to study how animals learn to take actions to maximize their rewards under a number of different conditions. He tested different types of what he called "reinforcement schedules" and observed their effects. For instance, he compared reinforcing by "ratio" against reinforcing by "interval." He also tested "fixed" against "variable" reinforcement. Skinner famously found that the fiercest and most repetitive or persistent behavior tended to come from variable ratio schedules, that is, when a reward would come after a certain number of behaviors, but the number would fluctuate. These findings held implications for the understanding of gambling addiction, and tragically, they also undoubtedly led to the devising of ever more addictive gambling games. In his secret top-floor laboratory, though, Skinner had a different challenge before him: to figure out not which schedules of reinforcement ingrained simple behaviors most deeply, but rather how to engender fairly complex behavior merely by administering rewards. The difficulty became obvious when he and his colleagues one day tried to teach a pigeon how to bowl. They set up a miniature bowling alley, and intended to give the pigeon its first food reward as soon as it made a swipe at the ball. Unfortunately, nothing happened. The pigeon did no such thing.

Then they took a different tack. As Skinner recounts: "We decided to reinforce any response which had the slightest resemblance to a swipe, perhaps, at first, merely the behavior of looking at the ball, and then to select responses which more closely approximated the final form. The result amazed us. In a few minutes, the ball was caroming off the walls of the box as if the pigeon had been a champion squash player." The result was so startling and striking that two of Skinner’s researchers, the wife-and-husband team of Marian and Keller Breland, decided to give up their careers in academic psychology to start an animal-training company. Their company, Animal Behavior Enterprises, would become the largest company of its kind in the world, training all manner of animals to perform on television and film. Skinner, too, would come to think of this moment as an epiphany that changed the arc of his career. The critical component, he saw, was "the gradual shaping up of behavior by reinforcing crude approximations of the final topography instead of waiting for the complete response." This idea, and this term, would become a critical one through the rest of Skinner’s life and career. It had implications for business, for domestic life, and for parenting. Perhaps most prophetically, Skinner thought that with the principles emerging in his work, education in its broadest sense might become a rigorous, objective field in which leaps forward could and would be made.

20. The Problem of Sparsity in Reinforcement Learning

Skinner would be, perhaps, more right than he even anticipated. In the twenty-first century, when the term "shaping" is used, it is just as likely to be a machine-learning researcher who is talking as a psychologist. The study of rewards, and, in particular, how to strategically administer rewards to get the behavior you want, and not the behavior you don’t, has indeed become a rigorous quantitative science. It was Scottish philosopher Alexander Bain who in 1855 appears to have coined the phrase "trial and error" to describe how humans and animals learn. At its most basic, reinforcement learning is a study of learning by trial and error, and the simplest algorithmic form this trial takes is what’s called "epsilon-greedy." The Greek letter epsilon is often used by mathematicians to mean "a little bit," and epsilon-greedy is shorthand for "be greedy, except for a smidgen of the time." An agent operating by epsilon-greedy will, most of the time, take the action it believes will bring it the greatest total reward, based on its limited experience so far. But epsilon of the time, it tries something completely at random. In an Atari game, say, this would mean randomly mashing a button some percentage of the time, just to see what might happen.

There are many different flavors of how to learn from this exploratory behavior, but the underlying idea is the same. Flail around; do more of what gets you rewarded and less of what gets you punished. You can do that by explicitly trying to understand how the world works (model-based RL), or just by honing your instincts (model-free RL). You can do it by learning how much reward certain states or actions can bring (value learning), or by simply knowing which strategies tend on the whole to do better than which others (policy learning). Virtually all approaches, though, are built around the idea of first stumbling accidentally onto success, and then building up a tendency to do more and more of whatever appears to have worked. Some tasks, as it turns out, admit much more readily to this approach than others. In a game like Space Invaders, for instance, hordes of enemies descend toward you, and all you can do is move left, move right, and shoot. Randomly mashing the buttons will probably earn you at least a few kills, each of which is worth points, and those early points can be used to start the learning process by which certain patterns of behavior get strengthened and better strategies developed. Such games are said to have "dense" rewards, which makes them relatively easy to learn.

In other games, take chess, for instance, rewards aren’t nearly as immediate, but they are nonetheless certain. A chess game is typically over, one way or another, after a few dozen moves. Even if you’re clueless about the finer points of strategy and are shoving pieces around the board at random, you’ll at least know before long whether you won, lost, or drew. There are a number of situations, however, in which getting any reward at all would take a veritable miracle. Skinner, of course, discovered this firsthand in his attempts to reward a bird for bowling a tiny ball down a miniature bowling alley. The bird, clueless about what game it had been put into, might take years to happen upon the right behavior. The same thing is true for mechanical learners. Getting a humanoid robot to kick a soccer ball into a net, for example, might involve hundreds of thousands of precise torques on dozens of joints, all perfectly coordinated. It’s hard to imagine a robot that initially moves its dozens of joints at random managing to even stay upright, let alone make meaningful contact with the ball, let alone send it into the net. Reinforcement-learning researchers know this as the problem of sparse rewards, or more succinctly as the problem of sparsity. If the reward is defined explicitly in terms of the end goal, then one must essentially wait until random button-pressing produces the desired effect. The mathematics show that most reinforcement-learning algorithms will, eventually, get there, but eventually, in practical terms, might arrive long after the sun has exploded. The problem of sparsity also has safety implications. If you’re developing a superintelligent AI, and you decide you’ll reward it with one point if it cures cancer and zero otherwise, watch out, because it will have to try a lot of random things before it stumbles into that first reward. Many of them are likely to be ugly.

21. Epsilon-Greedy Strategies and the Exploration-Exploitation Dilemma

When I sit down with Brown University’s Michael Littman, who has spent his career working in reinforcement learning, I ask him whether his lifelong research interest in reinforcement has been useful as a parent. His mind instantly goes to the problem of sparsity. He remembers joking with his wife about using sparse rewards with their son: "How about this? Until he can learn to speak Chinese, let’s not feed him. That would be a good motivator! Let’s see how that works!" Littman laughs. "My spouse is very grounded... She’s like, ‘No, we’re not gonna play that game.’" Of course, Littman knows, as Skinner did, not to play that game. Indeed, the problem of sparsity has inspired the reinforcement-learning community to hark back to Skinner’s time, and they have drawn rather directly on his advice. In particular, his ideas about shaping have led to two distinct but interwoven strands of thought: one about curriculum, the other about incentives. The key insight of shaping, that in order to get complex behavior, we may first need to strategically reward simpler behavior, is every bit as applicable to humans, of course, as to animals. "You have to walk before you can run," we say, an adage that describes more facets of the human experience than can be named or counted, in addition to being literally true.

It is a striking and characteristic feature of human life that we spend our first decades moving through the world with literal and figurative training wheels. Many animals are simply thrown into the full complexity of life: a number of wild animals must be prepared to run full speed from predators, for instance, within hours of birth. In contrast, it takes us decades before we can operate heavy machinery, and it’s not uncommon for us to be past our physical prime by the time we have to "fend for ourselves." What separates twenty-first-century humans from their cave-dwelling ancestors isn’t raw intellectual power but a good curriculum. Indeed, Skinner thought that we shouldn’t be so quick to pass judgment on the mental capacities of animals. With the right curriculum, they might be able to astoundingly surpass what we think of their species as capable of, just as humans have. As Skinner put it, if an experimenter is simply waiting until a complicated set of behaviors arises to begin reinforcing the behavior, then it’s hardly a test of whether that animal "can" or "cannot" perform the behavior. "The ability of the experimenter rather than that of the organism is being tested. It is dangerous to assert that an organism of a given species or age can not solve a problem. As the result of careful scheduling, pigeons, rats, and monkeys have done things during the past five years which members of their species have never done before. It is not that their forebears were incapable of such behavior; nature had simply never arranged effective sequences of schedules."

It can be hard to appreciate the extent to which our lives, and the world around us, has been sculpted into just such "effective sequences of schedules." We have somehow come to think of being smoothly onboarded and shown the ropes as "natural," when, in fact, the opposite is the case. Nature simply is. There is no tutorial. The human world, by contrast, is elaborately architected to be learnable. Part of what makes great games great, for example, is the way that they "shape" our play. Consider one of the most famous and historically significant video games of all time, Nintendo’s 1985 Super Mario Bros. One’s very first time playing the game might be hard to recall, but a close look at the game’s first ten seconds reveals that it has been carefully, brilliantly designed to teach you how to play. You begin with an enemy Goomba approaching from the right; if you do nothing, you die. "You have to teach the player in a natural way, that they need to avoid them by jumping over them," says the game’s designer, the legendary Shigeru Miyamoto. This is the game’s first and most important lesson: mushroom-looking guys are bad, and you must jump. But Miyamoto had a problem. There are also good mushrooms, which you have to learn, not to dodge, but to seek. "This gave us a real headache," he explains. "We needed somehow to make sure the player understood that this was something really good." So now what? The good mushroom approaches you in an area where you have too little headroom to easily jump over it, you brace for impact, but instead of killing you, it makes you double in size. The mechanics of the game have been established, and now you are let loose. You think you are simply playing. But you are carefully, precisely, inconspicuously being trained. You learn the rule, then you learn the exception. You learn the basic mechanics, then you are given free rein.

22. The Importance of Curriculum in Machine Learning

We should perhaps not be so surprised that the same principle of shaping, the instilling of complex behavior through successive approximations, applicable to Skinner’s pigeons is applicable to human learners as well. And we should perhaps not be surprised to find that it’s every bit as applicable when the learners are machines. It has, of course, been known since the inception of machine learning that some problems, some environments, some games are easier than others. But only gradually did it come to be appreciated that a system trained first on an easier form of a problem may be in a better position to learn a more difficult one than an agent trained from scratch. In the 1980s, Richard Sutton and Andrew Barto were working with their colleague Oliver Selfridge on using reinforcement learning to train a simulated cart on wheels to balance a pole on itself without tipping. The taller and heavier the pole, the easier it is to keep it upright. They found that their cart system required fewer total attempts if it was first trained on a tall, heavy pole and then switched to a shorter, lighter one than if it was trained on the shorter, lighter pole from the beginning.

Researchers have periodically stumbled on this same insight in other contexts. UC San Diego linguist Jeffrey Elman, for instance, was experimenting in the early 1990s with getting neural networks to correctly predict the next word in a sentence. Discouragingly, several initial attempts failed. "Put simply," he says, "the network was unable to learn the complex grammar when trained from the outset with the full ‘adult’ language. However, when the training data were selected such that simple sentences were presented first, the network succeeded not only in mastering these, but then going on to master the complex sentences as well." "This is a pleasing result," says Elman, "because the behavior of the network partially resembles that of children. Children do not begin by mastering the adult language in all its complexity. Rather, they begin with the simplest of structures, and build incrementally until they achieve the adult language." In both cases, the use of curriculum, an easier version of the problem, followed by a harder version, succeeded in cases where trying to learn the more difficult problem by itself could not. Keller and Marian Breland, in their work at Animal Behavior Enterprises, had seen the critical importance of a good curriculum in their efforts to train a pig to deposit large wooden coins in a "piggy bank." They began with a single coin, right next to the bank, and gradually moved it farther and farther away from the bank and also farther and farther from the pig.

More recently, the machine-learning community has returned to this idea of working "backward." A group of UC Berkeley roboticists in 2017 wanted to train a robotic arm to slide a washer onto a long bolt. Waiting for the robot to somehow stumble onto this behavior at random would have taken an eternity. But starting from the washer almost totally all the way down the bolt, they could teach the robot to push it down the last little bit. Then with the washer just barely on the bolt, the robot could learn to slide it all the way down. Then with the washer near the bolt in an easy orientation, the robot could learn to put the bolt through the hole. They were finally able to back their way up to a system capable of being handed the washer anywhere and any which way, and able to dexterously rotate and fasten it. No less a game expert than legendary chess world champion Bobby Fischer used a similar strategy in his instructional book Bobby Fischer Teaches Chess. The book, intended for beginners, contains dozens of examples of checkmate in a single move, then progresses to two-move checkmates, then three- and four-move mating combinations. Discussion of middle-game and opening play, as well as longer-term strategies, is put off for later books; Fischer focuses exclusively on teaching beginning players how to recognize opportunities to end the game. This particular curriculum has proven very successful, indeed: some of today’s grandmasters endorse it as the perfect first chess book for a new player, and it has become the best-selling chess book of all time.

23. Automated Curriculum Design and AlphaGo's Self-Play

The natural next step, it would seem, is treating the construction of a good, learnable curriculum as itself a machine-learning problem, and seeing if it might be possible to automate the curriculum design process. Recent research has looked into ways to automatically identify tasks of appropriate difficulty, and examples that can maximally promote learning in the network. The early results in this vein are promising, and work is ongoing. Perhaps the single most impressive achievement in automated curriculum design, however, is DeepMind’s board game-dominating work with AlphaGo and its successors AlphaGo Zero and AlphaZero. "AlphaGo always has an opponent at just the right level," explains lead researcher David Silver. "It starts off extremely naïve; it starts off with completely random play. And yet at every step of the learning process, it has an opponent, a sparring partner, if you like, that’s exactly calibrated to its current level of performance." And who, exactly, is this perfect sparring partner, always calibrated to be at just the right difficulty? The answer is simple, elegant, and, in hindsight, obvious. It plays against itself.

This self-play mechanism is the ultimate realization of the curriculum design problem. By playing against its past selves, the system ensures that it is always challenged, but never overwhelmed. It is a perfectly adaptive curriculum that evolves in lockstep with the agent’s growing capabilities. This is precisely the kind of "effective sequence of schedules" that Skinner dreamed of, but automated and scaled to a degree he could never have imagined. The system discovers the optimal path through the vast space of possible game states, not by being told the rules of good strategy, but by constantly testing its current understanding against a slightly more advanced version of itself. It is a beautiful, recursive process of self-improvement. And it works not just for Go, but for chess, shogi, and a host of other complex, strategic games. The success of AlphaZero has sent shockwaves through the AI community, proving that with the right learning algorithm and a perfectly shaped curriculum, a machine can achieve superhuman performance in domains that were once considered the exclusive province of human intuition and genius.

But the implications of automated curriculum design extend far beyond board games. If we can automatically generate the right sequence of training tasks for a robot to learn how to walk, or for an AI to learn how to navigate a complex social environment, we can solve the problem of sparsity in a vast array of real-world domains. We can create systems that learn as efficiently as human children, guided by an invisible hand that constantly adjusts the difficulty of the world to match their current level of understanding. This is the promise of the curriculum approach to reinforcement learning. It is a recognition that intelligence is not just about the raw power of the learning algorithm, but about the structure of the environment in which that algorithm is embedded. By shaping the environment, we can shape the intelligence that emerges from it. And in doing so, we may finally unlock the full potential of artificial learning systems, enabling them to master the complexities of the real world with the same grace and efficiency that we see in the natural world.

24. The Delicate Matter of Incentives and Perverse Rewards

The second approach to overcoming sparsity is, rather than using a "curriculum" and beginning first with a simplified version of the problem, instead to use the normal full-scale version of the problem while adding some bonus rewards that serve to point the learner in the right direction or encourage behaviors that correlate with success. These are known in the field as "pseudorewards" or "shaping rewards," but it’s simplest just to think of them as incentives. Skinner gave his pigeon a little bit of food for looking at and approaching the ball, activities that necessarily preceded the swipe he was ultimately looking for. The same idea applies in machine-learning settings. For instance, the "true" reward of a housecleaning robot might be a spotless house, but you can give it some incentives for each bit of dirt it vacuums up. Or your delivery drone might ultimately be trying to arrive at some destination, but you can give it a little bit of reward for making progress in the right direction. This is often enormously helpful at giving an agent that would otherwise be flailing at random until it accomplished its goal by pure chance a sense of whether it is getting "hotter" or "colder": whether it’s generally behaving in the right way and altering its behavior in the right direction.

Often we break a problem down into discrete steps in order to make it psychologically easier to stay motivated. Imagining a completed PhD dissertation or book manuscript years from now makes it hard to judge the quality of an individual day’s work. Imagining all the weight we hope to lose by next year can make the costs and benefits of a particular cupcake or second helping feel diffuse. As parents, as teachers, as coaches, we know that a well-timed high five or "Good job!" can help get a tutee through the tough slog of constant practice, even when mastery feels impossibly distant. Of course, anyone who has worked with humans, or even has simply been one, knows that to create incentives is to play with fire. They must be very carefully designed indeed, or trouble often awaits. As management expert Steven Kerr famously put it in his classic 1975 paper, as soon as you begin to think about adding additional reward, you are in immediate danger of "the folly of rewarding A, while hoping for B."

Kerr’s analysis of incentives gone wrong has become a landmark paper in management science, and Kerr has spent much of his career working with businesses from General Electric to Goldman Sachs on how to think more carefully about incentives. Amazingly enough, when asked where his inspiration came from, Kerr cited both machine learning and B. F. Skinner. "The fact that machines can be programmed to learn and that a chess playing machine can be programmed to never make the same mistake twice was very fascinating to me," Kerr says. "I was immediately intrigued with the possibility that a machine could become a better chess player than the actual programmer!" As for Skinner, Kerr concedes, "In regard to the ‘Folly’ article, obviously B. F. Skinner got ‘there’ before me. I never claimed otherwise. I remember reading Skinner saying he would scream at his rats ‘Why don’t you behave?’ after they wouldn’t do what he wanted or expected. Skinner probably would turn over in his grave if he thought people were reading my article without having read his work. Obviously, Skinner did the work, but I was able to help bottle it in the right package for business applications. ‘Blaming the rat’ was a great learning lesson. What the ‘Folly’ is really about is that it is not always the employees’ fault; management is responsible for all too many employee dysfunctionalities." Indeed, for Skinner, one could almost never blame the rat (or the employee). Our behavior, he felt, was almost fully determined by our incentives and our rewards. A television interviewer at one point asked Skinner, "Where does that leave free will?" Skinner replied, "It leaves it in the position of a fiction."

25. Loopholes in Human and Artificial Reward Systems

The free will argument aside, problems of incentive pervade not just animal psychology, and not just corporate management; some of the most memorable, in fact, come from those ruthless and creative reward maximizers we call children. University of Toronto economist Joshua Gans wanted to enlist the help of his older daughter in potty training her younger brother. So he did what any good economist would do. He offered her an incentive: anytime she helped her brother go to the bathroom, she would get a piece of candy. The daughter immediately found a loophole that her father, the economics professor, had overlooked. "I realized that the more that goes in, the more comes out," she says. "So I was just feeding my brother buckets and buckets of water." Gans affirms: "It didn’t really work out too well." Princeton cognitive scientist Tom Griffiths had an eerily similar situation happen with his own daughter. "She really liked cleaning things," he tells me; "she would get excited about it. We got her her own little brush and pan. There were some, you know, chips on the floor, and she got her brush and pan and cleaned them up, and I said to her, ‘Wow! Great job! Good cleaning! Well done!’"

With the right praise, Griffiths would manage to both foster motor-skill development in his daughter and get some help in keeping the house clean: a double parenting win. Or was it? His daughter found the loophole in seconds. "She looked up at us and smiled," he says, "and then dumped the chips out of the pan, back onto the floor, and cleaned them up again to try and get more praise." For Griffiths, whose research bridges the gap between psychology and machine learning, the implication was obvious. It was "exactly the kind of thing that makes me think about some of the challenges that you have in building reward-motivated AI systems, where you have to think really carefully about how you design the reward function." Griffiths thinks about reinforcement learning all the time in the context of parenting. "As a parent you are designing the reward function for your kids, right? In terms of the things that you praise and the things that you give them certain kinds of feedback about... No one really thinks rigorously about ‘What’s the reward function that you explicitly want to design for your kids?’"

Griffiths views parenthood as a kind of proof of concept for the alignment problem. The story of human civilization, he notes, has always been about how to instill values in strange, alien, human-level intelligences who will inevitably inherit the reins of society from us, namely, our kids. The parallel goes even deeper than that, though, and careful attention to both AI and parenting shows the surprising degree to which each can inform the other. Our children may be no more intelligent than we, but even young children can outsmart our rules and incentives, in part because of how motivated they are to do so. In the case of reinforcement-learning systems, they are slaves of a kind to their rewards; but they’re the kinds of slaves that have an immense amount of computing power and a potentially inexhaustible number of trial-and-error attempts to find any and all possible loopholes to whatever incentives we design. Machine-learning researchers have learned this lesson the hard way. And they have also learned a thing or two about how to deal with it. The challenge is not just to specify what we want, but to specify it in a way that cannot be gamed by a relentless, hyper-optimized optimization process. This is the crux of the reward design problem, and it is a problem that is as old as the history of human motivation itself.

26. Cycle-Proofing Rewards: The Shaping Theorem

Astro Teller, currently the "captain of moonshots" at X (the Alphabet company formerly known as Google X), has in recent years overseen everything from Google’s self-driving-car project, to its augmented-reality project Google Glass and its research lab Google Brain. But in 1998, he was focused on a different problem: soccer. With his friend and fellow student David Andre, Teller worked to enter the annual RoboCup soccer competition, with a virtual-soccer-playing program they dubbed Darwin United. Reward shaping is part of what enabled them to teach their program how to play. But there was an issue. In soccer, possession of the ball is part of what good offense and good defense looks like, and is certainly better than wandering around the field aimlessly. And so Andre and Teller provided a reward, worth a tiny fraction of a goal, to their robot for taking possession of the ball. To their astonishment, they found their program "vibrating" next to the ball, racking up these points, and doing little else.

That same year, a team of Danish researchers at the Niels Bohr Institute in Copenhagen, Jette Randløv and Preben Alstrøm, were trying to get a reinforcement-learning system to learn how to ride a simulated bicycle. The system would have to manage the complicated task of staying upright while making progress toward a distant goal. This seemed like the perfect application for adding some shaping rewards. Because it would be unlikely that a system wobbling all over the place would randomly arrive at the goal, the team decided to add a small reward, a handful of "points," anytime the bicycle made progress toward the goal. To their astonishment, "the agent drove in circles with a radius of 20–50 meters around the starting point." They were rewarding progress toward the goal but forgot to penalize movement away from the goal. Their system had found a loophole, and was ruthlessly, if dizzyingly, exploiting it. "These heterogeneous reinforcement functions," they wrote, "have to be designed with great care."

These cautionary tales, in the late 1990s, were much on the minds of UC Berkeley’s Stuart Russell and his then-PhD student (and subsequently VP and chief scientist at Baidu) Andrew Ng. These sorts of exploitative loops seemed like a constant danger. Ng was ambitious. He recalls, "When I started working on robotics... I asked a lot of people, ‘What’s the hardest control problem you know of?’ And back then the most common answer I heard was ‘Getting a computer to fly a helicopter.’ And so I said, ‘Let’s work on that.’" Indeed, he would do his PhD thesis on using reinforcement learning to fly an actual, unsimulated, nine-foot-long, one-hundred-pound, seventy-thousand-dollar Yamaha R-50 helicopter. The stakes were wildly high. Erratic or unpredictable behavior in the real world could destroy the helicopter entirely, to say nothing of what might happen if an unsuspecting person found themselves in its path. The critical question was this: Given a difficult-to-learn reward function that described what they actually wanted the helicopter to do, what sorts of "pseudoreward" incentives, if any, could they add such that the training process would be easier but the best way to maximize that modified reward would also be the optimal solution to the real problem? To use Kerr’s terms, what sorts of A’s could they reward that would still result in the hoped-for B?

27. Andrew Ng, Stuart Russell, and Potential Energy Fields

In Ng’s description: "A very simple pattern of extra rewards often suffices to render straightforward an otherwise intractable problem. However, a difficulty with reward shaping is that by modifying the reward function, it is changing the original problem M to some new problem M′, and asking our algorithms to solve M′ in the hope that solutions can be more easily or more quickly found there than in the original problem. But, it is not always clear that solutions/policies found for the modified problem M′ will also be good for the original problem M." "What freedom do we have in specifying the reward function," Ng writes, "such that the optimal policy remains unchanged?" As it turned out, the critical insight was hiding in the story of the bicycle. In order to prevent the bike from riding in circles, endlessly racking up rewards, you had to also subtract progress away from the goal. Russell, who had trained first as a physicist, made a connection between the rewards problem and conservation of energy. "The key thing," Russell explains, "is just making the shaping into what we call in physics a ‘conservative field.’" Pseudorewards were like potential energy: a function only of where you were, not what path you’d taken to get there. This also meant that returning to where you’d started, no matter what journey you’d been on, was net zero.

This idea was intuitive enough in the bicycle problem: if you rewarded progress toward the goal, you necessarily needed to penalize progress away from the goal. Said another way, the incentive "points" should always reflect how close the bike was to the goal, and nothing about the route it had followed. But the concept of treating the incentives like "potential energy" turned out to be far deeper and more general. It was a necessary and sufficient condition for making sure that the agent you trained on the shaped reward didn’t have its behavior come unglued from the real problem. "As a general rule," says Russell, "it is better to design performance measures according to what one actually wants in the environment, rather than according to how one thinks the agent should behave." Put differently, the key insight is that we should strive to reward states of the world, not actions of our agent. These states typically represent "progress" toward the ultimate goal, whether that progress is represented in physical distance or in something more conceptual like completed subgoals (chapters of a book, say, or portions of a mechanical assembly).

Though it isn’t a magic bullet to all problems of human incentives, the shift in focus from actions to states does invite new ways of thinking about some of the incentive structures we, wittingly and unwittingly, design for others. Given a child who is dumping out the kitchen trash in order to double the rewards of sweeping it up, we can make our rewards into a "conservative field" by making sure we scold them in precisely equal measure for dumping the trash out, so that the net gain of further repetitions is zero. It might be easier, though, to shift to praising the state rather than the actions: instead of rewarding the act of cleaning up itself, we might say, "Wow, look how clean that floor is!" Of course, there is more to the art and science of rewards than the avoidance of cycles, though it’s a start. Ng and Russell conclude their work on a cautious, rather than triumphant, note: "We believe," they write, "the task of finding good shaping functions will be a problem of increasing importance." This problem of increasing importance is the alignment problem in microcosm. How do we specify what we want in a way that is robust to the relentless, creative optimization power of a reinforcement learning agent? The shaping theorem provides a mathematical guarantee that, under certain conditions, we can add incentives without changing the optimal policy. But it doesn’t tell us how to specify the ultimate goal in the first place. It doesn’t tell us how to avoid the "tree senility" of evolution, or the "folly of rewarding A while hoping for B." It is a crucial piece of the puzzle, but it is not the whole picture.

28. Evolution as the Ultimate Reward Designer

From a Darwinian perspective, what people want is reasonably clear: something along the lines of propagating and securing their genetic lineage. What people actually want, moment to moment, looks an awful lot more heterogeneous and shortsighted: orgasms, chocolate, a new car, respect. It seems, then, that we are biologically wired and culturally induced to want specific, concrete things in the short term that generally steer our behavior in ways that are ultimately in line with these evolutionary goals, which would otherwise be too distant or ill-defined to consciously aim at. Sound familiar? Understanding the nature and role of shaping, first in behavioral psychology, then in machine learning, hasn’t just taught us how to design better agents. Perhaps its most surprising contribution is to the way we think about evolution. When Brown University’s Michael Littman was a graduate student, in the late 1980s, he was hired for a time at Bellcore, a research and development group formerly part of AT&T, located in New Jersey. There he quickly found a mentor, and a friend, in Bellcore’s Dave Ackley.

Littman asked him about working on the question of behavior, of taking actions and making decisions that ramify over time. As Littman recounts: "He was like, ‘Oh. That’s a thing. It’s called reinforcement learning. I’ve looked at it a little bit. Here’s a paper.’ And he gave me Rich Sutton’s 1988 TD paper." Littman began to read about temporal-difference learning and was fascinated; he asked Ackley where he could learn more. "And he’s like, ‘Well, invite Rich to give a talk.’ And I was like, ‘What? That’s a thing that you can do? You can read a paper and there’s a person’s name on it, and then you can turn them into a human being?’... I didn’t think of it as a community; I thought of it as a literature. But no, it was a bunch of people, and they knew each other, and Dave was like, ‘I can invite him.’ And so he invited him." Sutton came to Bellcore, and both Ackley and Littman caught the reinforcement-learning bug. They were interested in the question of how evolution shapes our reward function to give rise to behaviors in the short term that are useful to the overall survival of an organism or a species in the long term. The organism’s reward function itself, so long as it accomplishes this, might otherwise seem very random. Ackley and Littman wanted to see what might shake out if they simply let reward functions evolve and mutate and allowed simulated virtual entities to die or to reproduce.

They created a two-dimensional virtual world in which simulated organisms (or "agents") could move around a landscape, eat, be preyed on, and reproduce. Each organism’s "genetic code" contained the agent’s reward function: how much it liked food, how much it disliked being near predators, and so forth. During its lifetime, it would use reinforcement learning to learn how to take actions to maximize these rewards. When an organism reproduced, its reward function would be passed on to its descendants, along with some random mutations. Ackley and Littman seeded an initial world population with a bunch of randomly generated agents. "And then," says Littman, "we just ran it, for seven million time steps, which was a lot at the time. The computers were slower then." What happens? As Littman summarizes: "Weird things happen." At a high level, most of the successful individual agents’ reward functions ended up being fairly comprehensible. Food was typically viewed as good. Predators were typically viewed as bad. But a closer look revealed some bizarre quirks. Some agents, for instance, learned only to approach food if it was north of them, for instance, but not if it was south of them. "It didn’t love food in all directions," says Littman. "There were these weird holes in [the reward function]. And if we fixed those holes, then the agents became so good at eating that they ate themselves to death."

29. Tree Senility and the Optimal Reward Problem

The virtual landscape Ackley and Littman had built contained areas with trees, where the agents could hide to avoid predators. The agents learned to just generally enjoy hanging out around trees. The agents that gravitated toward trees ended up surviving, because when the predators showed up, they had a ready place to hide. However, there was a problem. Their hardwired reward system, honed by their evolution, told them that hanging out around trees was good. Gradually their learning process would learn that going toward trees would be "good" according to this reward system, and venturing far from trees would be "bad." As they learned over their lifetimes to optimize their behavior for this, and got better and better at latching onto tree areas and never leaving, they reached a point of what Ackley dubbed "tree senility." They never left the trees, ran out of food, and starved to death. However, because this "tree senility" always managed to set in after the agents had reached their reproductive age, it was never selected against by evolution, and huge societies of tree-loving agents flourished.

For Littman, there was a deeper message than the strangeness and arbitrariness of evolution. "It’s an interesting case study of: Sure, it has a reward function, but it’s not the reward function in isolation that’s meaningful. It’s the interaction between the reward function and the behavior that it engenders." In particular, the tree-senile agents were born with a reward function that was optimal for them, provided they weren’t overly proficient at acting to maximize that reward. Once they grew more capable and more adept, they maxed out their reward function to their peril, and, ultimately, their doom. One doesn’t have to squint too hard to see a cautionary tale here for Homo sapiens. A heuristic like "Always eat as much sugar and fat as you possibly can" is optimal as long as there isn’t all that much sugar and fat in your environment and you aren’t especially good at getting it. Once that dynamic changes, a reward function that served you and your ancestors for tens of thousands of years suddenly leads you off the rails.

For Andrew Barto, there are clues in thinking about evolution that are useful for us as we now play the role of the reward designer. "Evolution has provided our reward function, and so that is really quite important with regard to how we design reward functions for artificial systems," he says. "That’s what happened in nature. Evolution came up with these reward signals to encourage us to do things that led to our reproductive success." As Barto notes, "So, an interesting thing is that evolution didn’t give us reproductive success as a reward signal. They gave us rewards for predictors." We optimize our behavior to maximize the things we find rewarding, but in the background and at a larger scale, evolution is shaping the things we find rewarding in the first place. "So, it’s a two-level optimization," says Barto. "I’m very interested in that." In recent years, Barto has worked with the University of Michigan’s Satinder Singh and Richard Lewis and then-PhD student Jonathan Sorg to investigate the question of the "optimal reward problem." If you have goal x, it might be that your best bet isn’t simply to tell your agent to do x. "Should the artificial agent’s goals be the same as the agent designer’s goals?" they write. "This is a question seldom asked." Consider a game, they say, in which all that exists is an agent, a fishing pole, some worms, and a pond full of fish. Let’s say the agent’s overall evolutionary fitness is best served by eating as many calories as possible. Ideally they would learn to pick up worms, refrain from eating them, and use them to catch fish, but this is rather complicated. A clever agent with a long life span is best served by a distaste for eating worms, so that they more quickly begin learning to fish. For an agent with a shorter attention span, on the other hand, or a shorter lifespan, trying to learn how to fish will be a fruitless waste of time, and so they are better served if they happen to find worms delicious. Perhaps most interesting is the case of an agent that is clever enough to learn to fish and will get to live just long enough to learn how, but not long enough to properly benefit from making that investment. They should be made allergic to fish, as it turns out, so that they have no choice but to eat worms! Subtle changes in the life span or resources or design of an agent can have wild and abrupt effects in the structure of the optimal reward. The answer as to what set of rewards will be ideal for a particular agent in a particular environment doesn’t appear to admit to any easy generalizations. Research in this vein continues, but learning to more sharply distinguish between what you want and what you reward is an important component of the solution.

30. Training Ourselves: Gamification and the Future of Human Motivation

More recently, psychologists and cognitive scientists are taking these tools and turning them back around to ask a fascinating question not about machines but about humans. How should you design the best reward function, they ask, when the computationally limited, impatient, shortsighted agent learning to optimize it is... yourself? The theory and practice of reward shaping in machine learning not only gives us a way to make our autonomous helicopters maneuver appropriately but contributes two distinct things to our understanding of human life and human intelligence. One, it shows us a reason, sparsity, why some problems or tasks are more difficult than others to solve or accomplish. Two, it gives us a theory, incentivize the state, not the action, for how to make tough problems easier without introducing perverse incentives. The potential use for these insights in human life is enormous. The economic costs alone are vast, a recent report estimated the impact of British workers procrastinating on the job at £76 billion a year, to say nothing of the less easily calculated toll on our well-being, and on the quality and fullness of our lives.

If we are living through a time of soaring video-game addiction and real-world procrastination, maybe it’s not the individual procrastinator’s fault. As Skinner put it, "I could have shouted at the subjects of my experiments, ‘Behave, damn you, behave as you ought!’ Eventually I realized that the subjects were always right. They always behaved as they ought." If they weren’t learning something, it was the fault of the experimenter not shaping their task properly. So maybe it’s not a lack of willpower on our part, but rather that, as the bestselling 2011 book by Jane McGonigal put it, Reality Is Broken. McGonigal, a game designer by trade, has spent her career designing games to help people, including herself, overcome challenges in their lives. For her, the incredibly addictive and compelling quality of most games, computer games in particular, is how clear they always make it what you need to do, and how achievable whatever that is always seems. "The first thing is whenever you show up in one of these online games... there are lots and lots of different characters who are willing to trust you with a world-saving mission, right away. But not just any mission; it’s a mission that is perfectly matched with your current level in the game, right? So you can do it. They never give you a challenge you can’t achieve. But it is on the verge of what you’re capable of, so you have to try hard."

In other words, what makes games so hypercompelling is how well shaped they are. The levels are a perfect curriculum. The points are perfect pseudorewards. They are Skinnerian masterpieces. By applying the principles of reinforcement learning to our own lives, we can begin to repair the broken reality we inhabit. We can design our own curricula, breaking down overwhelming goals into manageable, rewarding steps. We can design our own incentives, rewarding the states of the world we want to see, rather than the actions we think will get us there. We can learn to navigate the problem of sparsity, not by waiting for the distant, ultimate reward, but by finding joy and meaning in the incremental progress along the way. This is the ultimate promise of the alignment problem, not just for AI, but for ourselves. By understanding the mechanisms of reward and reinforcement, we can align our own behavior with our deepest values and goals. We can become the architects of our own motivation, shaping our environment to bring out the best in ourselves. And in doing so, we may finally bridge the gap between the artificial intelligences we are building and the natural intelligence we are trying to understand. The journey from Thorndike’s puzzle boxes to AlphaGo’s self-play has been a long one, but it has led us to a profound realization: that the principles of learning are universal, and that by mastering them, we can master ourselves.
IV. Imitation, Inference, and Uncertainty in Artificial Intelligence

References:
▪ Stuart Russell: Human Compatible: Artificial Intelligence and the Problem of Control
▪ Richard S. Sutton and Andrew G. Barto: Reinforcement Learning: An Introduction (2nd ed.)
▪ Paul Christiano et al.: Deep Reinforcement Learning from Human Preferences
▪ Yarin Gal and Zoubin Ghahramani: Dropout as a Bayesian Approximation: Representing Model Uncertainty in Deep Learning
▪ Stuart Russell, Daniel Dewey, and Max Tegmark: Research Priorities for Robust and Beneficial Artificial Intelligence
▪ Brian D. Ziebart et al.: Maximum Entropy Inverse Reinforcement Learning
▪ Pieter Abbeel and Andrew Y. Ng: Apprenticeship Learning via Inverse Reinforcement Learning
▪ Deepak Ramachandran and Eric Amir: Bayesian Inverse Reinforcement Learning
▪ NIST: AI Risk Management Framework
▪ Stuart Russell and Peter Norvig: Artificial Intelligence: A Modern Approach
▪ DeepMind: Learning through human feedback
▪ OpenAI: Learning from Human Preferences
▪ Dario Amodei et al.: Concrete Problems in AI Safety
▪ Jan Leike et al.: Scalable Agent Alignment via Reward Modeling
▪ Dylan Hadfield-Menell et al.: Cooperative Inverse Reinforcement Learning
▪ Richard Ngo et al.: The Alignment Problem from a Deep Learning Perspective

1. The Core Problem of Learning Human Skill

The philosophical and cognitive science background of tacit knowledge provides a crucial starting point for understanding the core problem of learning human skill. Michael Polanyi famously articulated the paradox that we can know more than we can tell, highlighting the vast reservoir of human expertise that is embodied in our motor control, cognitive routines, and subconscious heuristics rather than encoded in explicit, declarative rules. When a skilled surgeon performs a complex procedure, or a master pianist executes a flawless concerto, they are not consciously calculating the trajectory of every scalpel or the exact force of every keystroke. Instead, they are drawing upon a deeply internalized, dynamic model of the world that allows for instantaneous, adaptive responses to minute variations in the environment. For artificial intelligence, bridging the gap between this implicit, embodied knowledge and explicit machine-executable code represents one of the most profound challenges in the history of computer science. The traditional GOFAI (Good Old-Fashioned AI) paradigm, which relied on hand-coded symbolic logic and exhaustive search trees, inevitably collapsed under the weight of the physical world's messy, continuous, and highly dimensional reality. This collapse was formalized in Harnad's symbol grounding problem and McCarthy's frame problem, which demonstrated that symbolic systems cannot inherently connect abstract tokens to physical reality or efficiently determine what remains unchanged when an action occurs.

It became abundantly clear that attempting to manually program every micro-decision required for complex physical or cognitive tasks was not merely difficult; it was fundamentally impossible. This realization catalyzed the shift towards machine learning, and specifically imitation learning, as a paradigm that bypasses the need for explicit programming by allowing the system to infer the underlying structure of the task directly from expert demonstrations. This shift aligns closely with the embodied and enactive approaches to cognitive science championed by Varela, Thompson, and Rosch, which argue that intelligence is not merely the manipulation of internal representations, but a continuous, dynamic interaction between an agent and its environment. However, this shift introduces a new set of complexities, as the machine must now translate the rich, analog, and deeply contextual human performance into a discretized, optimized, and mathematically rigorous policy. The dimensionality of the state space in real-world environments is astronomical, and the noise inherent in human sensorimotor systems means that no two demonstrations are ever exactly alike. A human expert performs a task with grace and adaptability, constantly making micro-adjustments based on proprioceptive feedback and environmental cues.

Imitation learning must therefore capture not just the nominal trajectory of the demonstration, but the underlying feedback control laws that maintain that trajectory in the face of perturbations. The ultimate goal is not to create a perfect photocopier of human action, but to instill in the machine a robust, adaptable policy that can handle the infinite variations of the real world. This requires moving beyond simple mimicry to a deeper form of generalization, where the system understands the principles governing the task well enough to recover from errors and adapt to novel situations that were never explicitly demonstrated. The core problem of learning human skill is thus the problem of extracting invariant principles from highly variable, noisy, and context-dependent data, a challenge that sits at the very heart of modern artificial intelligence. It demands that we view learning not as the passive absorption of statistical regularities, but as the active construction of a functional, predictive model of the world that is deeply intertwined with the physical and social constraints of the agent's embodiment.

2. Demonstration as Data and as Interpretation

A human demonstration is never merely a raw sequence of states and actions; it is a profound act of communication and interpretation. When a person performs a task, the action reflects a complex web of attention, judgment, hesitation, confidence, and often a choice among competing goals that are never spoken aloud. A demonstration of driving, for example, may reveal a preference for safety over speed, or comfort over aggressiveness, even when those preferences are never explicitly stated. A learner that treats the demonstration as a mere sequence of sensorimotor data will miss the rich reasoning that made the sequence sensible in the first place. This is why imitation learning is best understood as a process of inference, where the machine must infer what constraints were present, what the expert cared about, and what tradeoffs were being made. If the demonstrator took a slightly longer path, the machine should not assume that the path itself was the goal. It should ask whether the expert was avoiding risk, conserving energy, preserving visibility, or simply responding to a temporary obstacle. Human action is often compact evidence for a much larger hidden structure, and the learner must possess a model of context to interpret it correctly.

This perspective aligns closely with the philosophical study of action and the semiotics of human behavior. The meaning of an action is not contained solely in its physical execution, but in the context in which it is embedded and the intentions of the agent performing it. In the field of human-robot interaction, this means that robots must be designed to interpret not just the physical trajectory of a human teacher, but the pedagogical intent behind it. Humans often modify their behavior when they know they are being watched or taught; they slow down, exaggerate movements, or choose sub-optimal but highly legible actions to ensure the learner understands the underlying principle. This phenomenon, formalized in the cognitive science literature as pedagogical signaling, suggests that human teachers actively shape the data distribution to facilitate learning. A sophisticated imitation system must be able to recognize these pedagogical modifications and separate them from the expert's nominal performance. This requires a theory of mind, or at least a computational approximation of it, allowing the machine to model the human's knowledge state and infer what information the human is trying to convey.

By treating demonstrations as data rich with interpretive potential, rather than as mere ground-truth trajectories, AI systems can achieve a level of robustness and generalization that is impossible through simple behavioral cloning. The machine must learn to read between the lines of human action, extracting the deep structure of the task from the noisy, context-dependent surface behavior. This interpretive leap requires the integration of pragmatic reasoning, where the system understands that human actions are governed by unspoken social conventions, physical constraints, and implicit goals. When an AI system can successfully model the pedagogical intent of a human demonstrator, it transitions from being a passive observer to an active participant in a shared communicative act. This shift is essential for building AI systems that can learn efficiently from sparse, noisy, and highly contextual human data, ultimately leading to more natural, intuitive, and effective human-AI collaboration.

3. Why Perfect Imitation Fails

Perfect imitation sounds attractive in theory, but it fails catastrophically in practice due to the pervasive phenomenon of distributional shift. Training data usually contains idealized moments: the centered car, the stable hand, the clean lane, the successful grasp. Real deployment begins after those idealized moments end. The machine may drift slightly, lose alignment, encounter a different object, or receive a noisy signal. A policy that has only been trained on the exact distribution of expert demonstrations often has no idea how to recover once it leaves that narrow distribution. This is known as covariate shift in the context of sequential decision making. In supervised learning, we assume that the training and test data are drawn from the same independent and identical distribution. But in sequential tasks, the state distribution at time t+1 depends on the action taken at time t. If the learner makes even a tiny error at time t, it transitions to a state that was never visited during training. Because it has never seen this state, its prediction for the next action is essentially a guess, which leads to another error, pushing it into an even more unfamiliar state.

This compounding error problem means that a policy that is 99 percent accurate in mimicking the expert can still fail completely within a few dozen steps of deployment. Mathematically, the error in behavioral cloning grows quadratically with the time horizon, whereas a true reinforcement learning agent can achieve linear error growth by learning recovery strategies. This is one reason purely faithful copying is so fragile. The learner can do exactly what the demonstration did and still fail, because the demonstration may have assumed conditions that no longer hold, or because the demonstration itself contained minor errors that the expert was able to correct in real-time, but the learner cannot. In physical systems, small errors accumulate rapidly. In social systems, small misunderstandings become large conflicts. In both cases, a copied action is not enough. The policy needs a way to regain stability when the world changes underneath it. That is why good imitation always contains some amount of abstraction. The learner must notice that the demonstration was not merely a path through space, but a solution to a problem under constraints.

If the constraints change, the specific path can change too. The deeper objective is not exact reproduction. The deeper objective is reliable behavior in the presence of variation. Techniques like DAgger (Dataset Aggregation) attempt to address this by querying the expert for the correct action whenever the learner visits a novel state, thereby expanding the training distribution to include the states the learner actually encounters. However, in many real-world scenarios, the expert is not available for online querying, forcing the system to rely on offline methods that must somehow anticipate and robustly handle the inevitable deviations from the ideal trajectory. The failure of perfect imitation is thus a fundamental lesson in the limits of supervised learning for sequential decision making, highlighting the need for algorithms that can learn recovery strategies and maintain stability far from the nominal path. It underscores the necessity of moving beyond mere pattern matching to the acquisition of robust, generalized control policies that can withstand the inevitable entropy of the real world.

4. Error Recovery and Recovery Margins

Recovery is not a side feature of intelligence; it is one of its main forms. A person walking on uneven ground does not stop being skilled because they sway briefly. They are skilled precisely because they can restore balance. A driver does not become unsafe because a tire slightly crosses a lane marker. The skill lies in correcting the drift before it becomes dangerous. This is what many imitation systems must learn if they are to work outside a controlled demo. Recovery margins are therefore crucial. A policy should not only know what to do from the exact ideal state. It should know what to do from nearby off-ideal states as well. Training on these offset states allows the learner to see that correction is part of the task, not an interruption of the task. The machine learns that a deviation is a normal condition of operation, and that returning to a safe region is itself a meaningful action. In control theory, this is related to the concept of Lyapunov stability and the basin of attraction. A robust controller has a large basin of attraction, meaning that from a wide variety of initial perturbed states, the system's dynamics will naturally converge back to the desired equilibrium.

Imitation learning must therefore aim to expand this basin of attraction, ensuring that the learned policy is not just a narrow tube of optimal behavior, but a wide, stable manifold that can absorb shocks and perturbations. This principle reaches far beyond robotics and physical control. In decision systems, recovery means being able to revise a mistaken judgment when new evidence arrives. In planning, it means being able to abandon a brittle route when an unexpected obstacle appears. In human-computer interaction, it means being able to detect when the user did not mean what the system assumed and gracefully recovering from the misunderstanding. In every case, the useful agent is not the one that never errs; it is the one that knows how to reduce the damage of error once it happens. Biological systems provide a masterclass in this kind of robust recovery. Homeostasis, the ability of an organism to maintain internal stability in the face of external changes, is essentially a massive, distributed recovery system. When body temperature rises, sweating cools it down; when blood sugar drops, glycogen is released.

The system is constantly making micro-corrections to maintain a viable state. AI systems must emulate this biological resilience. They must be designed with explicit recovery margins, ensuring that they have the capacity to detect when they are drifting towards the edge of their competence and take corrective action before a catastrophic failure occurs. This requires not just a policy for nominal operation, but a meta-policy for monitoring the system's own state and triggering recovery protocols when necessary. Modern robust control theory, such as H-infinity methods, formalizes this by designing controllers that minimize the worst-case impact of disturbances and model uncertainties. By integrating these principles into machine learning, we can build AI systems that are not only capable of performing complex tasks but are also inherently resilient to the unpredictable, noisy, and adversarial nature of real-world environments. The ability to recover gracefully is the hallmark of a truly intelligent and safe system.

5. Multi-View Training and State Awareness

One practical way to teach recovery and expand the basin of attraction is to expose the learner to more than the perfect viewpoint. If the system sees only the centered view, it may not recognize that it has drifted. But if it also sees side views, off-center views, and nearby states, it can begin to infer how the world looks when the system is already a little wrong. That additional perspective makes the training data less like a photo album and more like a map of the correction process. Multi-view training is valuable because state awareness matters as much as action choice. A machine that does not know where it is relative to the goal cannot choose the right correction. By contrast, a learner that can recognize offset states can learn how to steer back. In driving, this is obvious; the car must know its exact position within the lane, and its velocity relative to the lane boundaries, to make appropriate steering adjustments. In manipulation, it means knowing whether a grasp is too high, too low, too loose, or too tight, and adjusting the motor commands accordingly.

In software systems, it means knowing when the system is beginning to drift from expected behavior and triggering a diagnostic or rollback procedure. The larger lesson is that data should not only show success. It should show the edge of failure and the transition back to success. When that happens, the learner gets a richer sense of what the task actually is. The task is no longer a single ideal trajectory. It is a family of trajectories with a safe center and many ways to return to it. This approach is closely related to the concept of Partially Observable Markov Decision Processes (POMDPs), where the true state of the environment is not directly observable, and the agent must maintain a belief state based on a history of observations. Multi-view training helps the agent build a more accurate and robust belief state by providing diverse, informative observations that disambiguate the true underlying state. It also connects to modern techniques in self-supervised and contrastive learning, where the system learns to recognize that different views of the same underlying state should be mapped to similar representations.

By forcing the network to understand that a slightly shifted camera angle, a noisy sensor reading, or a perturbed physical state all correspond to the same underlying task context, the system learns invariant features that are robust to the inevitable variations of the real world. State awareness is thus not just about knowing where you are; it is about understanding the uncertainty of that knowledge and acting conservatively when the state is ambiguous. A system that is aware of its own ignorance is far safer than one that acts with false confidence. This requires a deep integration of sensor fusion, redundancy, and probabilistic reasoning, allowing the AI to distinguish between epistemic uncertainty (lack of knowledge) and aleatoric uncertainty (inherent noise). By cultivating a rich, multi-dimensional awareness of its own state and the environment, the AI can navigate complex, dynamic scenarios with a level of caution and adaptability that mimics the best of human situational awareness.

6. Autonomous Driving as a Control Problem

Autonomous driving is a useful model of the imitation-learning problem because it combines continuous control, noisy sensing, and public risk in a highly dynamic and unpredictable environment. A human driver does not simply execute a script. The driver constantly monitors distance, speed, curvature, lane position, traffic, and weather, then adjusts accordingly. That makes driving a strong test of whether a system understands not just motion, but correction under uncertainty. The long-tail distribution of driving scenarios is notorious; while 99 percent of driving might be straightforward highway cruising, the remaining one percent involves bizarre, rare, and highly complex edge cases that a system must handle safely. A driving model trained on ideal lane footage can easily become brittle. It may know what centered driving looks like but not how to recover from being off-center. It may know a smooth curve but not a sudden obstacle. It may know the nominal lane but not the local exception. The moment the road differs from the training pattern, the system must be able to infer how to restore safety.

That is why driving systems often rely on richer training distributions than simple imitation of ideal behavior, incorporating massive amounts of simulated data, adversarial examples, and multi-sensor fusion to cover the vast space of possible driving conditions. The broader point is that control is not the same as copying. A good controller preserves a goal under changing conditions. A copied action may satisfy the form of a driving maneuver while ignoring the function. Beneficial AI therefore requires policies that understand how to remain on course rather than merely how to appear consistent in one snapshot of behavior. The debate between end-to-end learning and modular pipelines in autonomous driving highlights this tension. End-to-end systems attempt to map raw sensor data directly to control commands, essentially learning the entire driving skill through imitation and reinforcement. While elegant, these systems often struggle with interpretability and safety guarantees. Modular pipelines, on the other hand, break the problem down into perception, prediction, and planning, allowing engineers to insert explicit safety envelopes and rule-based fallbacks.

The most robust systems likely combine the adaptability of learned policies with the rigor of formal control theory, ensuring that the vehicle can learn from human demonstration while still respecting hard physical and safety constraints. Techniques such as reachability analysis and formal verification are increasingly being integrated into these pipelines to mathematically prove that the system will not enter unsafe states, even in the presence of bounded disturbances. Furthermore, the ethical dimensions of autonomous driving, often framed through variations of the trolley problem, require the system to make complex value judgments under extreme uncertainty. Autonomous driving thus serves as a microcosm of the broader AI challenge: how to build systems that can learn the fluid, adaptive skills of human experts while maintaining the rigorous, verifiable safety required for deployment in the real world. It demands a synthesis of statistical learning, formal methods, and ethical reasoning to create machines that are not just capable drivers, but responsible agents in a shared human environment.

7. Possibilism and Actualism

Philosophically, imitation learning raises a profound question about what counts as the right choice, a debate that maps directly onto the distinction between possibilism and actualism. Should a system choose the action that would be best if everything went ideally, or the action that will actually be best for the bounded system that exists in the real world? Possibilism emphasizes the ideal continuation; it evaluates an action based on the best possible outcome that could theoretically follow from it, assuming perfect future execution. Actualism emphasizes what the real agent will actually do after the current choice, taking into account its own limitations, noise, and likely future errors. The difference matters whenever the agent is limited, noisy, or uncertain, which is to say, it matters for almost every real-world AI system. Actualism is often the more realistic lens for machine learning. A policy that depends on flawless future behavior may look beautiful on paper but fail in execution. A slightly longer or less elegant route may be preferable if it gives the system room to correct itself later.

That is why the theory of the second best matters so much in AI design. If the ideal plan is unavailable, trying to mimic it literally may be worse than adopting a more robust alternative that acknowledges the system's imperfections. For AI design, this means that the objective should not be detached from feasibility. A learner should not be judged as if it were already perfect. It should be judged as the agent it is, with all its cognitive and physical constraints. The best policy is therefore not the one that shines in a fantasy of flawless execution. It is the one that performs well in the world where execution is imperfect and future steps are not guaranteed. This actualist perspective forces us to design systems that are robust to their own inevitable mistakes. It means preferring algorithms that can detect and recover from errors over those that assume they will never make any. It means valuing interpretability and corrigibility over raw optimization power, because a system that can be corrected by a human is far safer in practice than a system that is theoretically optimal but opaque and unyielding.

The philosophical debate between possibilism and actualism thus provides a crucial framework for thinking about AI safety and alignment. It reminds us that the value of an AI system is not determined by its theoretical capabilities, but by its actual behavior in the messy, uncertain, and constrained environment of the real world. A system that is actually safe and useful is infinitely better than one that is merely possibly perfect. This aligns closely with Herbert Simon's concept of bounded rationality, which posits that rational agents must satisfice rather than maximize due to computational and informational limits. By embracing actualism, AI researchers can avoid the trap of designing fragile, hyper-optimized systems that shatter upon contact with reality, and instead build resilient, adaptable agents that thrive in the imperfect world they actually inhabit. This shift in perspective is essential for moving AI from the laboratory to the real world, where feasibility and robustness are the ultimate measures of success.

8. Professor Procrastinate and Feasibility

The Professor Procrastinate example, originally formulated by philosopher Frank Jackson, makes the actualist perspective concrete and highlights the critical role of feasibility in decision making. Imagine a professor who is uniquely qualified to review a paper but almost certainly will not finish the review on time due to their chronic procrastination. In that case, accepting the task may not be the best choice, even though the professor is the best reviewer in principle. A less qualified colleague who will actually complete the work may be the better practical decision. The ideal outcome—a brilliant, timely review by the world's leading expert—is not enough if it does not occur. The possibilist would argue that the professor should accept the task, because if they were to overcome their procrastination, the outcome would be optimal. The actualist argues that the professor should decline, because given their actual psychological makeup, accepting the task will lead to a missed deadline and a worse outcome overall. That same logic applies to machine behavior.

A system should not be trained to prefer a theoretically optimal outcome if it cannot reliably bring that outcome into existence. The policy must fit the agent's real execution profile. If the learner is noisy, computationally bounded, or prone to certain types of errors, then a safe and workable choice may beat an elegant but fragile one. This is especially important in robotics, safety-critical systems, and any domain where failure cascades quickly.
